Title: Mouse 1.0 Design Notes
ID: 6985ba07-04f4-8396-9d84-67b0814d29bf

User: In this chat I will finally ask you to code a python program simulating the movements of a mouse in a cage. However, before really coding , we will develop an algorithm defining the movements of a mouse skeleton. The skeleton will be defined by a graph consisting of body part nodes connected by skeleton edges. Compared to a real mouse the skeleton will be over simplified. But it will have enough potential to relate to a computer graphics hull/mesh of a voluminous mouse. But this will not be part of the system initially (but part of mouse 2.0).

Part of Mouse 1.0 will be a random 3D track in a mouse cage of rectangular size (program parameters —cage-height, —cage-width). The path will initially be located on the ground plane z=0 of the cage, and it will „wiggle“ through the cage with random directional changes not making it too crooked. So direction changes may in intensity be controlled by a parameter, but they depend on cage size also (I would think now). Speed of the movement randomly varies from 0 to —max-speed.

Initially, the movements of the mouse follow 2 behavior classes: 0 running, and 1 rearing. Over time parameter t, behavior switches from one class to the other randomly. Running lets it walk as described above. Rearing we define here as lifting the front paws off the ground for some shorter time (moving clumsily on the hint paws).

The mouse graph will be read from a text file —mouse-graph mouse.txt. It contains the labels of the graph nodes and its 3D coordinates in a balanced initial zero motion position with coordinates in a local body coordinate system. There will also be a center/reference position that allows to relate the body model to the cage model. The file contains a nodes table (with body part positions and reference position), and an edges table (with skeleton edges).

Next I will define the motion pattern of the behaviors. But I first want to submit the text and allow you to make first comments.

Assistant: Thanks — this is a solid Mouse 1.0 spec. Here are the main design notes and pitfalls I’d address *before* you start encoding behavior-specific motion.

## 1) Separate “locomotion in the cage” from “pose of the skeleton”
You effectively have two layers:

1) **World trajectory**: a time-parameterized rigid-body transform  
   - position `P(t) ∈ R³` (in cage coordinates)  
   - orientation `R(t)` (heading/yaw at minimum; optionally pitch/roll later)

2) **Local pose**: the skeleton’s joint/node positions expressed in the mouse’s local body frame, then transformed by `R(t), P(t)` into world coordinates.

This separation prevents you from mixing “where the mouse is” with “how it’s posed,” and it makes Mouse 2.0 (mesh/hull) much easier: the mesh just follows the same rigid-body transform + per-node pose offsets.

## 2) The graph file should be explicit about coordinate frames and a reference
You mention a “center/reference position.” Make that a first-class concept.

**Recommendation**: in the file, designate:
- `root` (reference node) — e.g., `pelvis` or `body_center`
- a **forward axis convention** in local coordinates (e.g., +X is forward, +Y left, +Z up), even if it’s implicit in your initial coordinates
- optionally, a named node for “ground contact baseline” (hind paws)

### Minimal file structure suggestion
Keep it easy to parse and hard to misinterpret:

- Header / metadata:
  - `units = cm` (or arbitrary)
  - `root = pelvis`
  - `forward_axis = +x`
  - `up_axis = +z`

- Nodes table:
  - `label, x, y, z`
  - include all body parts as nodes
  - **do not** store the “reference position” separately if it is simply the root node; just store the root as a node.

- Edges table:
  - `labelA, labelB`
  - You may later want to attach edge attributes:
    - stiffness, rest_length (derivable), max_angle, etc.

### Critical constraint
Your initial node coordinates should be in a *balanced* pose where “grounded nodes” (hind paws, maybe belly) have `z=0` in local coordinates, *or* you explicitly specify which nodes are ground contacts and what local ground plane means.

Otherwise rearing vs running becomes ambiguous.

## 3) Trajectory generation: don’t “random walk in position,” random-walk the *heading/curvature*
To avoid “too crooked,” it’s much better to generate motion using a **bounded-curvature** process:

- Maintain a yaw angle `θ(t)` and yaw rate `ω(t)`
- Evolve `ω(t)` with noise + damping (Ornstein–Uhlenbeck is ideal), clamp to `|ω| ≤ ω_max`
- Speed `v(t)` evolves similarly (noise + damping), clamp to `[0, max_speed]`
- Update:
  - `θ(t+dt) = θ(t) + ω(t)*dt`
  - `P(t+dt) = P(t) + v(t)*dt * [cos θ, sin θ, 0]`

This automatically produces “wiggle, but not too crooked” because curvature is controlled by `ω_max` and the noise scale.

### Boundary handling inside a rectangle
Naively clamping `x,y` at walls causes ugly sticking.

Better options (in increasing sophistication):
1) **Reflect heading** when predicted step crosses boundary (specular reflection).
2) **Wall repulsion field**: add a steering term that turns away from walls as distance decreases.
3) Combine both: repulsion most of the time, reflection only for “would cross next step.”

Given your “depends on cage size” intuition: make `ω_max` and steering gains scale with cage dimensions, e.g. using the smaller dimension `L = min(width, height)` so behavior stays similar across cages.

## 4) Time and behavior switching: use a continuous-time state model (not per-step coin flips)
If you flip behavior each frame with probability `p`, the expected duration depends on `dt` and becomes fragile.

Use **sojourn times**:
- When entering a behavior, sample its duration from a distribution (exponential, log-normal, uniform in a range).
- Run it for that duration, then switch and resample.

This makes behavior stable under different sampling rates.

## 5) Rearing should be modeled as a pose modulation + optional locomotion degradation
Your rearing definition: “lifting front paws off ground, moving clumsily on hind paws.”

So conceptually:
- The *trajectory layer* changes slightly:
  - speed reduces (lower mean, more variance, maybe more pauses)
  - turning becomes noisier or less stable (or the opposite, depending on what you want)
- The *pose layer* changes:
  - spine pitches up (local rotation around lateral axis)
  - front paw nodes lift in +Z, maybe retract toward chest
  - center-of-mass shifts backward (pelvis root shifts slightly)
  - hind paws remain near ground plane

Even with a graph-only skeleton (no joint angles), you can implement rearing as **procedural offsets**:
- Choose a small set of “control nodes” (pelvis, spine_mid, neck, head, front_paw_L/R, hind_paw_L/R)
- Apply smooth time functions to their local positions during rearing (ease-in/out), then propagate to other nodes if needed.

Later, Mouse 2.0 could replace that with a constrained IK system, but for 1.0 procedural offsets are enough.

## 6) Graph vs kinematic tree: decide early how you’ll handle constraints
A general graph can have cycles, which complicates posing.

For Mouse 1.0 you’ll want one of:
- Treat it as a **tree** rooted at `root`, with edges representing bones (preferred for posing).
- Or allow general graph but internally extract a spanning tree for transforms.

If you keep it as a graph, define what an edge *means*: rigid distance constraint? just a visual segment? something in-between?  
For a procedural skeleton, the simplest meaning is: **edges are for drawing and for rest-length constraints** (optional).

## 7) Determinism: include a seed and ensure reproducible tracks
If you want experiments / debugging:
- accept `--seed`
- make trajectory + behavior sampling come from one RNG stream (or named streams)

## 8) Parameter sanity list (what you’ll almost certainly need)
Even if you don’t expose all of them initially, design as if they exist:

- `dt` (simulation time step)
- `max_speed`
- speed dynamics: mean, noise scale, damping
- turn dynamics: `omega_max`, noise scale, damping
- cage: width, height
- wall avoidance: repulsion distance, gain
- behavior durations: run duration distribution params, rear duration params
- rearing pose: lift height, spine pitch, easing time

## 9) A small conceptual trap: “speed randomly varies from 0 to max” can look jittery
If speed is independently resampled each step, motion becomes twitchy.

Better: treat speed as a smooth stochastic process (noise + damping) and clamp to `[0, max_speed]`. Then you get natural accelerations without writing a full biomechanics model.

---

If you want, the next step (still before coding) is to formalize two things on paper:

1) **Exact mouse-graph file grammar** (so parsing is unambiguous).  
2) **Mathematical definition of the trajectory generator** (state variables, update equations, boundary handling).  

Once those are fixed, the behavior layer becomes “just” parameter modulation + pose offsets, and implementation is straightforward.

Assistant: Good — with a tree and a fixed local coordinate convention, you can keep Mouse 1.0 *very* simple and still get convincing-ish motion later by swapping in better pose functions.

Below is a minimalist, *procedural* way to define the two behaviors in terms of **(A) world rigid motion** + **(B) local node offsets**.

---

## Core representation (use for both behaviors)

Let the mouse-graph file define **rest positions** in local coords:

- `p_i0` for each node `i` (head, paws, tail…)
- `p_ref = (0,0,0)` is your reference point at z=0 below the head
- root node is `head`

At time `t`, define:

- world position `P(t) = (x(t), y(t), z(t))` (for Mouse 1.0, `z(t)=0`)
- yaw `θ(t)` from the trajectory generator
- rotation `Rz(θ)` about world z-axis

Then world node position is

`p_i_world(t) = P(t) + Rz(θ(t)) * ( p_i0 + Δp_i(t) )`

Where `Δp_i(t)` is a **behavior-dependent local offset** (often zero).

This keeps things clean: the track determines `P(t), θ(t)`; behaviors only define `Δp_i(t)` and optionally modulate speed/turn noise.

---

## Behavior 0: running (as simple as possible)

### Minimal option (almost no pose)
- **All offsets zero**: `Δp_i(t)=0` for all nodes.
- Mouse is a rigid “stick figure” moving along the 2D path.
- This already validates: parsing graph, transforms, boundary handling, speed variations.

### Slightly less stiff (still trivial): add 2–3 cheap oscillations
You can add tiny periodic offsets that don’t try to enforce contact.

Define a phase that advances with speed:

`φ(t+dt) = φ(t) + 2π * (v(t)/stride_len) * dt`

Then:

**(1) head bob (optional)**
- `Δp_head.z = A_head * sin(φ)`
- maybe `Δp_nose.z = A_nose * sin(φ + phase_shift)`

**(2) front paws alternate**
- left front: `Δp_LFP.z = A_paw * max(0, sin(φ))`
- right front: `Δp_RFP.z = A_paw * max(0, -sin(φ))`

**(3) hind paws alternate (phase shifted)**
- left hind: `Δp_LHP.z = A_paw * max(0, sin(φ + π))`
- right hind: `Δp_RHP.z = A_paw * max(0, -sin(φ + π))`

**(4) tail sway**
- tail tip: `Δp_tail_tip.y = A_tail * sin(φ + ψ_tail)`
- optionally a smaller sway for tail root

That’s it. No IK, no ground contact enforcement, but it reads as “alive.”

Parameters you’d expose later:
- `stride_len` (sets cadence)
- `A_head, A_paw, A_tail` (small)

---

## Behavior 1: rearing (simple, defined as a timed pose transition)

Rearing is best treated as a **state with duration** `T_rear`, with an internal normalized time `u ∈ [0,1]`:

`u = clamp((t - t_start)/T_rear, 0, 1)`

Use a smooth ease function (so it doesn’t snap). Example:

`e(u) = 3u² - 2u³`  (smoothstep)

Now define rearing offsets as scaled by `e(u)`:

### Pose changes (local offsets)
**Front paws lift**
- `Δp_LFP.z = H_front * e(u)`
- `Δp_RFP.z = H_front * e(u)`
Optionally retract them:
- `Δp_LFP.x = -X_retract * e(u)`
- `Δp_RFP.x = -X_retract * e(u)`

**Head rises a bit**
- `Δp_head.z = H_head * e(u)`
- `Δp_nose.z = (H_head + H_nose_extra) * e(u)`

**Shift weight backward (very cheap COM hint)**
- move tail root slightly down/back:
  - `Δp_tail_root.x = -X_back * e(u)`
  - `Δp_tail_root.z = -Z_down * e(u)` (small)

**Hind paws stay “grounded” in local pose**
- simplest: keep hind paw offsets at ~0, or even slightly press them:
  - `Δp_LHP.z = -H_press * e(u)`
  - `Δp_RHP.z = -H_press * e(u)`

**Tail counterbalance**
- tail tip goes down/back or sideways a little:
  - `Δp_tail_tip.x = -X_tail * e(u)`
  - `Δp_tail_tip.z = -Z_tail * e(u)`
  - optional mild y sway

### Locomotion modulation during rearing
Keep it simple:
- reduce speed: `v_rear = α * v_run` with `α ~ 0.1…0.4`
- optionally increase turning noise a bit (clumsy): `ω_noise *= 1.2`

---

## How behavior switching should work (stable under dt)
For each state, sample a duration:
- running: `T_run ~ Uniform(a,b)` or log-normal
- rearing: `T_rear ~ Uniform(c,d)` (short)

No per-frame coin flips.

---

## One important consistency check with your “reference point”
You said: reference point is below head at **Z=0**, and `p_ref=(0,0,0)`.

That implies:
- head node `p_head0.z > 0`
- hind paws should likely have `z≈0` (or close), otherwise “ground plane” in local space is unclear.

If your node coordinates don’t have that property, it’s still fine — but then define which nodes represent “ground contact height” in local space and adjust once at load time.

2) If oscillations: which nodes get (z-bob, paw-lift, tail-sway) and approximate amplitudes  
3) Whether stride phase follows speed (`φ` from `v`) or is a fixed frequency

Then we can lock an exact behavior definition and move straight into coding.

Assistant: Got it. We can make this very simple, deterministic, and still consistent.

## Running: trot + rigid ears–nose triangle + small head motion

### 1) Keep the ears + nose rigid by treating them as a “head rigid cluster”
Instead of giving independent offsets to `nose`, `left_ear_tip`, `right_ear_tip`, define a **single small rigid transform** applied to all three (and optionally to `head` itself).

Let the “head cluster” nodes be:
- `head`, `nose_tip`, `left_ear_tip`, `right_ear_tip`

At time `t`, define a *local* head transform relative to rest pose:
- a small translation `d_head(t)` (optional)
- a small rotation `Q_head(t)` (quaternion or rotation matrix), e.g. tiny pitch/roll/yaw oscillations

Then for any node in the cluster:
`p_i_local(t) = d_head(t) + Q_head(t) * p_i0`

This guarantees the triangle (ears–nose, with head as anchor) stays rigid.

**Small head oscillation** (simple):
- `pitch(t) = A_pitch * sin(2π f_head t + φ0) + noise`
- `roll(t)  = A_roll  * sin(2π f_head t + φ1) + noise`
(keep these amplitudes tiny)

### 2) Trot gait (diagonal pairs)
Trot means diagonal pairs move together:
- Pair A: left front + right hind
- Pair B: right front + left hind

Define stride phase `φ(t)` tied to speed:
`φ(t+dt) = φ(t) + 2π * (v(t)/stride_len) * dt`

Define a “lift” signal that is zero for half the cycle and positive for the swing phase:
`lift(φ) = max(0, sin(φ))`

Then:
- `Δz(LFP) = A_paw * lift(φ)`
- `Δz(RHP) = A_paw * lift(φ)`
- `Δz(RFP) = A_paw * lift(φ + π)`
- `Δz(LHP) = A_paw * lift(φ + π)`

Optionally add tiny fore-aft swing too (still simple):
- `Δx(paw) = -A_swing * cos(φ)` for the swinging pair, and opposite for the stance pair (or just omit; z-only already reads as gait).

This is “toy gait” (no contact constraints), but it will visually look like trot.

---

## Rearing: up then down, aiming for head–tailroot edge ~ vertical

You don’t need the “e(u)” concept at all. Use a **single bell-shaped envelope** that starts at 0, rises to 1 mid-rear, returns to 0:

Let `u = (t - t_start)/T_rear` clamped to `[0,1]`.  
Define:
`h(u) = sin(π u)`  → 0 at u=0, 1 at u=0.5, 0 at u=1

That is the whole “up then down” time law.

### 1) Make the body “rear” by pitching the body axis
Define the body axis in local coordinates as the vector from `tail_root` to `head` in the rest pose:
`a0 = p_head0 - p_tail_root0`

We want that axis to become ~vertical (parallel to +z) at peak.

Compute the needed pitch angle (in local space around the local y-axis, because +x is forward and +z up):
- project `a0` into the x–z plane: `a0_xz = (a0.x, 0, a0.z)`
- current angle from +x toward +z is `α0 = atan2(a0.z, a0.x)`
- vertical corresponds to `α_target = π/2`
- required pitch change: `Δα = α_target - α0`

Then during rearing, apply a pitch rotation to a chosen “body cluster” around a pivot near `tail_root` (or your reference point):
`pitch(u) = Δα * h(u)`

Body cluster for rearing (suggested):
- `head`, `nose_tip`, `ears`, `tail_root`, `tail_tip`
- hind paws stay near ground (either excluded from this pitch, or included with extra correction)

Apply:
`p_i_local(t) = pivot + Ry(pitch(u)) * (p_i0 - pivot)`

### 2) Front paws lift and then return
While the body pitches, also lift/retract front paws with the same envelope:
- `Δz(front_paw) = H_front * h(u)`
- `Δx(front_paw) = -X_retract * h(u)` (optional)

### 3) Keep hind paws on/near the ground plane (simple correction)
If pitching the body cluster pulls hind paws off z≈0, do a cheap correction:
- after computing local positions, set hind paw z to 0 (or to their rest z) during rearing:
  - `p_LHP.z = 0`, `p_RHP.z = 0`
This is nonphysical but stable and readable.

### 4) Individuality (small randomness, but controlled)
At behavior start, sample per-rear scalars:
- `H_front *= (1 + ε1)`, `Δα *= (1 + ε2)`, small `ε ~ Uniform(-0.1,0.1)`
- tiny asymmetry: add `±δ` to left/right front paw lift

---

## Cameras: 3 orthogonal views + exact 2D keypoints from projection

Assume cage coordinates:
- x: cage width direction
- y: cage depth direction
- z: up

Cage spans:
- `x ∈ [0, W]`, `y ∈ [0, D]`, `z ∈ [0, Hc]` (even if mouse stays on z=0)

### Camera extrinsics (defaults)
Let cage center `C = (W/2, D/2, Hc/2)`.

1) **Cam1 (top-down)**  
   Position: `E1 = (W/2, D/2, Z1)` with `Z1 > Hc`  
   Look-at: `C`  
   Up vector: `(0,1,0)` (so image x-axis aligns with cage x)

2) **Cam2 (front view)** (looking along +y toward the cage)  
   Position: `E2 = (W/2, -Y2, Z2)` with `Y2 > 0`, `Z2 ~ Hc/2…Hc`  
   Look-at: `C`  
   Up: `(0,0,1)`

3) **Cam3 (side view)** (looking along +x)  
   Position: `E3 = (-X3, D/2, Z3)`  
   Look-at: `C`  
   Up: `(0,0,1)`

### Projection model
Use a standard pinhole camera:
- intrinsics: focal length `fx, fy`, principal point `(cx, cy)`, image size `(px_w, px_h)`
- compute camera coordinates: `p_cam = R * (p_world - E)`
- project:  
  `u = fx * (p_cam.x / p_cam.z) + cx`  
  `v = fy * (p_cam.y / p_cam.z) + cy`

For Cam1 (top-down), you can also use **orthographic** to keep it super simple and distortion-free. But pinhole is fine as long as you set distance/FOV so the whole cage fits.

### Guarantee “whole cage visible”
For each camera, choose distance (or FOV) so that the cage’s 8 corners project inside the frame with some margin. Programmatically:
- project all 8 cage corners
- adjust focal length or camera distance until all are within `[0..w-1]×[0..h-1]`

This makes setup robust for any cage size.

---

## Outputs: video + 2D keypoints + 3D ground truth

### Video (10 fps)
- one video file per camera: e.g. `cam1.mp4`, `cam2.mp4`, `cam3.mp4`
- draw skeleton edges as lines; optionally draw node markers

(Implementation later: OpenCV `VideoWriter` with `fps=10`.)

### 2D keypoints (no noise)
Store per camera per frame:
- `frame_idx, time, behavior, node_label, u, v, visible`
(visible=false if behind camera or outside image)

CSV is easiest; JSON lines also fine.

### 3D coordinates
Store per frame:
- `frame_idx, time, behavior`
- per node: `x,y,z` in world coords
Optionally also store:
- `P(t)` (mouse reference position), `yaw θ(t)`, and if rearing uses pitch: `pitch(t)`.
