Assistant: You’re seeing two *different* issues:

1) **Rearing stretches the front legs** because the current rearing code **moves the head (via body pitch) but then assigns front paw positions independently**, so the `head–front_paw` edge length is not preserved.

2) In your `mouse2.txt`, the paws are **not on the local ground plane** (`z=-8`), despite the earlier assumption “all paws have z=0”. That means any code that pins paws to `z=0` will inevitably create large apparent leg-length changes. In `mouse2.txt` both front paws and hind paws have `z=-8`. fileciteturn0file0

---

## A) Fix rearing so `head–front_paw` length stays constant and paws lift off ground

The clean Option-A fix (no extra joints) is:

- Pitch the body around `tail_root` as before (to make `tail_root→head` near vertical at peak).
- Then place each front paw by **rotating its rest leg vector around the head**, choosing the rotation so the paw reaches a desired **absolute z** (above ground), while **preserving the head–paw distance** exactly.

### 1) Add this helper (near your math helpers)

```python
def rotate_leg_vec_about_y_to_reach_z(v0: np.ndarray, z_rel_target: float) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    Rotate vector v0 around the local y-axis (using the same x-z convention as roty)
    so that the rotated vector's z component equals z_rel_target (as closely as possible),
    while preserving vector length. y component is unchanged by y-rotation.

    Returns rotated vector v'.
    """
    x = float(v0[0]); y = float(v0[1]); z = float(v0[2])
    R = math.hypot(x, z)
    if R < 1e-12:
        return v0.copy()

    # Clamp to reachable range in x-z plane
    zt = max(-R, min(R, z_rel_target))

    # We have z' = -sin(b)*x + cos(b)*z = R * cos(b + beta0)
    # where beta0 satisfies cos(beta0)=z/R, sin(beta0)=x/R  => beta0 = atan2(x, z)
    beta0 = math.atan2(x, z)
    ang = math.acos(zt / R)

    b1 = -beta0 + ang
    b2 = -beta0 - ang

    def xprime(b: float) -> float:
        return math.cos(b)*x + math.sin(b)*z

    # Choose the solution that retracts more (smaller x')
    b = b1 if xprime(b1) < xprime(b2) else b2

    xp = math.cos(b)*x + math.sin(b)*z
    zp = -math.sin(b)*x + math.cos(b)*z
    return np.array([xp, y, zp], dtype=np.float64)
```

### 2) Replace the front-paw assignment in `pose_rearing_tight()`

Find the current block:

```python
    # Front paws lift and retract during rearing; keep y fixed; z rises then falls
    for paw in (lfp, rfp):
        p0 = g.nodes[paw]
        nodes[paw] = np.array([p0[0] - rear_paw_retract * h, p0[1], rear_paw_lift * h], dtype=np.float64)
```

Replace it with this:

```python
    # Front paws: preserve head->paw length by rotating the rest leg vector around head.
    head_pos = nodes[head]  # head after body pitch

    # Desired absolute paw height above ground during rearing (0..rear_paw_lift..0)
    paw_z_abs = rear_paw_lift * h

    for paw in (lfp, rfp):
        v0 = g.nodes[paw] - g.nodes[head]  # rest vector from head to paw (constant length)
        z_rel_target = paw_z_abs - float(head_pos[2])  # desired z relative to head
        v_rot = rotate_leg_vec_about_y_to_reach_z(v0, z_rel_target)
        nodes[paw] = head_pos + v_rot
```

Result:
- Front paws **actually lift off** the ground (because `paw_z_abs > 0` for `h>0`).
- `||paw - head||` stays **constant** for both front legs during rearing.

### 3) Make sure your rearing pitch uses the corrected “vertical at peak” formula
Keep the *sign-correct* pitch computation we discussed earlier (the `pitch_peak = atan2(-ax, az)` version).

---

## B) Your `mouse2.txt` violates “paws at z=0” (and that matters)

In `mouse2.txt`, paws are at `z=-8` (front and hind), while your reference ground is `z=0`. fileciteturn0file0  
So:

- Running code that forces paws to `z=0` will **move paws 8 units upward**.
- With Option A’s simplified gait, that will *look like leg length changes* (because it *is* changing in the current running implementation).

If you truly want the model’s “neutral pose” to be grounded, edit the file so paws are on the ground plane:

- `left_front_paw ... 0`
- `right_front_paw ... 0`
- `left_hind_paw ... 0`
- `right_hind_paw ... 0`

That alone will make both running and rearing look less “stretchy”.

---

## C) Unit switch cm→mm can make trot motion look tiny (if you didn’t scale speeds/noises)

Your trot amplitude is tied to:

- `stride_len = z_head/3`
- paw x amplitude ≈ `stride_len/4`

If you switched the mouse model to mm but **did not** scale:
- `--max-speed`, `--speed-noise`, `--wall-repulse-dist`, and cage dimensions

then everything becomes effectively “10× smaller/ slower” relative to the cage, and the gait will look almost rigid.

Rule of thumb: if you multiply all lengths by 10 (cm→mm), also multiply:
- `--max-speed` and `--speed-noise` by 10
- `--wall-repulse-dist` by 10
- cage dimensions by 10

Angles / fps stay unchanged.

User: Now the rearing works. And I corrected the mouse2.txt. 

But why is this?

    def paw_x_offset(phase: float) -> float:
        return (stride_len / 4.0) * tri_wave(phase)

Does it really make sure that on a straight path one paw is moving twice as fast as the overall body and one paw is fixed to the ground?

Assistant: It’s not arbitrary. That exact combination

- **phase advance**: `dφ/dt = 2π * v / stride_len`
- **triangle wave** (piecewise linear from +1 → −1 over half a cycle)
- **amplitude**: `stride_len / 4`

is chosen so that, on a **straight path with constant yaw**, you get the kinematics you described **in the forward direction**:

- stance paw: **world forward velocity ≈ 0**
- swing paw: **world forward velocity ≈ 2v**

### Why it works (derivation)

On a straight path, ignore yaw (or treat it constant), and look only along the local forward axis `x`.

For one paw, the simulator effectively does:

\[
x_{\text{paw,world}}(t) = x_{\text{body}}(t) + x_{\text{paw,rest}} + \Delta x(\varphi(t))
\]

where

\[
\Delta x(\varphi) = \frac{\text{stride\_len}}{4}\,\text{tri\_wave}(\varphi)
\]
and
\[
\frac{d\varphi}{dt} = 2\pi \frac{v}{\text{stride\_len}}
\]

Now look at the **stance half-cycle**, where `tri_wave` goes linearly from `+1` to `−1`. In that region it has constant slope:

\[
\frac{d\,\text{tri\_wave}}{d\varphi} = -\frac{2}{\pi}
\]

So:

\[
\frac{d\,\Delta x}{dt}
= \frac{\text{stride\_len}}{4}\cdot\left(-\frac{2}{\pi}\right)\cdot \frac{d\varphi}{dt}
= \frac{\text{stride\_len}}{4}\cdot\left(-\frac{2}{\pi}\right)\cdot 2\pi \frac{v}{\text{stride\_len}}
= -v
\]

Therefore the paw’s world forward velocity is:

\[
\frac{dx_{\text{paw,world}}}{dt} = v + (-v) = 0
\]

That is your “paw fixed to ground” (in forward x).

In the **swing half-cycle**, the triangle wave slope flips sign:

\[
\frac{d\,\Delta x}{dt} = +v
\Rightarrow
\frac{dx_{\text{paw,world}}}{dt} = v + v = 2v
\]

So yes: with that amplitude and phase law, the paw alternates between “fixed” and “twice speed” **along the forward axis**, for straight motion.

### What it does *not* guarantee
- It does **not** keep the leg edge length constant (unless you do the rearing-style leg rotation trick for running too). Your running gait currently overwrites paw positions directly.
- It does **not** keep the paw fixed in full 2D when the path curves (you’d need per-paw world “locks” in stance and let the body move around them, i.e., a different controller).
- It makes “fixed” only in the *idealized* continuous case; with discrete frames (dt = 0.1 s at 10 fps) you’ll see small stepping artifacts.

### Why it may look tiny in `mouse2.txt`
With your corrected `mouse2.txt`, `head.z = 27` (in whatever length unit you’re using). fileciteturn1file0  
Then `stride_len = z_head/3 = 9`, and the paw oscillation amplitude is `stride_len/4 = 2.25`.

If your cage is, say, hundreds of mm wide, a 2.25 mm paw excursion will indeed look almost rigid. That’s expected from the formula, not a bug.

**Practical fix**: introduce a multiplier, e.g.
- `stride_len = stride_scale * z_head/3` (try `stride_scale = 2…5`)

If you want, I can give you the minimal patch adding `--stride-scale` (and also a running-mode option that preserves head–paw and tailroot–hindpaw edge lengths by rotating the leg vectors, similar to the rearing fix).

User: Can I enforce straight paths (just for checking) ?

Assistant: Yes. For a “pure straight-line sanity check” you want **(a)** constant heading, **(b)** no steering/wall-avoid turning, and ideally **(c)** constant speed. You can do that either **without code changes** (good enough) or with a **tiny flag** (best).

## Option 1: straight enough without changing code
Turn off all yaw dynamics and steering, and (optionally) speed dynamics:

```bash
python mouse_sim.py --mouse-graph mouse2.txt --write-video --duration 5 --fps 10 --seed 2 --out-dir out_straight \
  --omega-max 0 --omega-noise 0 --omega-damp 0 --wall-repulse-gain 0 \
  --speed-noise 0 --speed-damp 0
```

Notes:
- Heading will be whatever the RNG picked initially; it will then stay constant.
- Speed will stay at its initial value (since you set noise/damp to 0).
- It will still **reflect** at walls if it reaches one; keep `--duration` short or use a big cage.

If you want it to go along +x specifically, you need Option 2.

## Option 2: enforce a straight path deterministically (recommended)
Add three CLI args and a tiny conditional.

### 1) Add arguments in `build_argparser()`
```python
p.add_argument("--straight-path", action="store_true",
               help="Force constant heading (no turning/steering).")
p.add_argument("--theta0", type=float, default=None,
               help="Initial yaw (radians). If set, overrides random init.")
p.add_argument("--v0", type=float, default=None,
               help="Initial speed. If set, overrides random init.")
```

### 2) Use `theta0` / `v0` when initializing `TrackState`
Replace:
```python
st = TrackState(
    x=...,
    y=...,
    theta=rng.uniform(-math.pi, math.pi),
    v=rng.uniform(0.0, args.max_speed),
    omega=0.0
)
```

with:
```python
theta_init = rng.uniform(-math.pi, math.pi) if args.theta0 is None else args.theta0
v_init = rng.uniform(0.0, args.max_speed) if args.v0 is None else args.v0

st = TrackState(
    x=args.cage_width * 0.5,
    y=args.cage_depth * 0.5,
    theta=theta_init,
    v=v_init,
    omega=0.0
)
```

### 3) In the main loop, bypass turning when `--straight-path` is set
Right before calling `step_track(...)`, do:

```python
if args.straight_path:
    # optional: keep speed constant too
    if args.v0 is not None:
        st.v = args.v0
    st.omega = 0.0

    # no turning: move straight, still clamp/reflect if you hit walls
    st.x += st.v * dt * math.cos(st.theta)
    st.y += st.v * dt * math.sin(st.theta)

    # keep same boundary handling as step_track
    if st.x < 0.0 or st.x > args.cage_width:
        st.theta = math.pi - st.theta
        st.x = min(max(st.x, 0.0), args.cage_width)
    if st.y < 0.0 or st.y > args.cage_depth:
        st.theta = -st.theta
        st.y = min(max(st.y, 0.0), args.cage_depth)
else:
    st = step_track(...)
```

### Run: straight along +x with constant speed
```bash
python mouse_sim.py --mouse-graph mouse2.txt --write-video --duration 5 --fps 10 --out-dir out_straight \
  --straight-path --theta0 0.0 --v0 100 \
  --omega-max 0 --omega-noise 0 --omega-damp 0 --wall-repulse-gain 0 \
  --speed-noise 0 --speed-damp 0
```

That gives you an ideal straight-line test case for verifying the trot math (stance ≈ fixed, swing ≈ 2v) before you re-enable curvature.

User: Okay, you are right. Trot works. It is visible when paths are straight and in addition to your command --max-speed 10 is set. Then motion is slow enough to visually check. Sorry for not believing it.

Please provide the --stride-scale fix as proposed above. the step length could be longer.

Assistant: Here’s the **minimal `--stride-scale` patch** (for your current script version with protocol + tightened rearing).

### What it does
It multiplies the automatically derived stride length:

- old: `stride_len = z_head / 3`
- new: `stride_len = stride_scale * (z_head / 3)`

This increases the **step length** (paw x excursion) and also **slows cadence** for a given body speed (because phase advance uses `v/stride_len`). That’s usually what you want when steps are “longer”.

---

## 1) Add the CLI arg in `build_argparser()`

Add this near the running gait options:

```python
p.add_argument(
    "--stride-scale",
    type=float,
    default=1.0,
    help="Multiply default stride length (z_head/3) by this factor."
)
```

---

## 2) Apply it where `stride_len` is computed in `simulate()`

Find:

```python
z_head = float(g.nodes[_norm_label("head")][2])
stride_len = z_head / 3.0 if z_head > 0 else args.stride_len_fallback
```

Replace with:

```python
z_head = float(g.nodes[_norm_label("head")][2])
base_stride = (z_head / 3.0) if z_head > 0 else args.stride_len_fallback
stride_len = args.stride_scale * base_stride
```

That’s it.

  --stride-scale 2

or more pronounced:

```bash
python mouse_sim.py --mouse-graph mouse2.txt --write-video --duration 30 --fps 10 --out-dir out_stride4 \
  --stride-scale 4
```

If you later decide you want **bigger paw excursion without changing cadence**, that’s a different knob (separate “amplitude scale” decoupled from stride length). But for “step length could be longer”, `--stride-scale` is the right first fix.

User: A mouse model including the files shown on the screenshot, with which software can it be read or visualized? I know that .ply can be read by meshlab and 3DSlicer. .tga seems to contain the surface that can be wrapped around the ply mesh. But which software combines them such that the ply contains the tga fur image etc.?

Assistant: What you have there is a typical “same asset exported to multiple formats” bundle. The **texture only gets “combined” with the mesh if the format supports materials + UVs + an external image reference (or embedding)**.

### Which file actually pairs with the `.tga`?
Almost certainly **the `.obj` + `.mtl` + `.tga` trio**:

- **`mouse.obj`**: mesh geometry *and* (usually) UV coordinates
- **`mouse.mtl`**: material file that tells the OBJ which image to use (e.g. `map_Kd mouse_01.tga`)
- **`mouse_01.tga`**: the image texture (diffuse/albedo)

The `.ply` file generally **does not** carry a standard “material + texture image filename” link. PLY is great for geometry (and sometimes vertex colors), but textured materials are not its strong point.

### Best software to view “mesh + texture” correctly
#### Easiest free option: **Blender**
Blender imports OBJ and will usually hook up the MTL automatically.

**How to make it work reliably:**
1. Put `mouse.obj`, `mouse.mtl`, and `mouse_01.tga` in the **same folder**.
2. In Blender: **File → Import → Wavefront (.obj)**.
3. In the import options, enable **Image Search** (helps Blender find the TGA).
4. Switch to **Material Preview** mode to see textures.

If the texture still doesn’t show:
- Open **Shading** workspace and check the material nodes.
- Ensure there is an **Image Texture** node loaded with the TGA connected to **Base Color**.
- Ensure the mesh has a UV map (OBJ usually does).

#### Also works well
- **Autodesk 3ds Max**: reads `.max` natively; also imports OBJ/FBX/3DS.
- **Autodesk Maya**, **Cinema 4D**, **Houdini**: import OBJ/FBX with textures.
- **Unity / Unreal Engine**: import FBX/OBJ (materials may need quick fixing).
- **Windows 3D Viewer / macOS Preview (USDZ etc.)**: sometimes works for FBX/OBJ, depending on OS/version.

### What about the `.fbx` and `.3ds`?
- **FBX** is often the best “single-file-ish” interchange: it can include UVs, materials, and sometimes embed textures (or reference them). Many tools load it nicely.
- **3DS** is older and has more limitations; sometimes materials/texture paths get lost.

### Why you’re not seeing “fur” in some viewers
Be careful with the word “fur”:
- If it’s **just an image** of fur, that’s the TGA on UVs → OBJ+MTL (or FBX) will show it.
- If it’s **actual groom/hair rendering** (common in V-Ray scenes), that typically lives in the **`.max`** files (especially the `*_vray.max`) and **will not survive** export cleanly to OBJ/PLY. In that case, what you’ll see in OBJ/FBX is only the base mesh + maybe a diffuse texture, not true fur strands.

### Practical recommendation
- For “mesh + texture image”: use **`mouse.obj` (with `mouse.mtl` + `mouse_01.tga`)** in **Blender**.
- If you want “as-authored” look (especially V-Ray materials/hair): open the **`.max`** in **3ds Max** (with V-Ray installed if required).
- If you need a modern portable textured format: import in Blender, then export **glTF (.glb)**.

If you want, paste the contents of `mouse.mtl` (it’s small), and I can tell you exactly which texture slots it expects (diffuse only vs normal/spec/etc.) and which file names/paths need to match.

User: Can we write a python program that reads .obj or .ply (and .mtl and .tga optionally) and the 3D body part position (output of Mouse 1) and a .txt file with the node coordinates (equivalent to the existing mouse.txt) of the mouse body parts (but from the .obj objects coordinate system). And then renders the mouse body from the cameras view points generating 3 much more realistic videos? We may need to extend mouse-sim.py by an interface file containing the cams parameters that is also input.

Assistant: Yes. Architecturally it’s straightforward:

1) **Load the textured mesh** (`.obj` + `.mtl` + `.tga` if present, or `.ply` without texture).
2) **Load the per-frame 3D body-part positions** (your `coords_3d.csv` from Mouse 1).
3) **Load a “mesh-coordinate skeleton”** (`mouse_mesh_nodes.txt`: same node labels, but coordinates expressed in the mesh’s coordinate system).
4) For each frame, compute a **rigid transform** that maps the mesh-coordinate skeleton onto the simulated skeleton positions.
5) Apply that transform to the mesh and **render from the 3 cameras**, producing 3 realistic videos.

The only nontrivial part is (4): in your current Mouse 1.0, paws are not kinematically consistent (they were “overwritten” to satisfy gait/rearing), so you **must not** fit the rigid transform using all nodes indiscriminately, or the solver will “compromise” and the mesh will wobble oddly.

### The key trick: fit the rigid transform using a rigid subset
Use only nodes that are rigid-ish relative to the torso, e.g.
- `head`, `tail_root`, `nose_tip`, `left_ear_tip`, `right_ear_tip`, `tail_tip`
and **exclude paws** (`*_paw`) from the fit.

Then the mesh follows the torso/head correctly, while paws can remain whatever they are in the skeleton overlay (or you ignore them for fitting).

---

## Recommended file interfaces

### 1) `mouse_mesh_nodes.txt`
Same labels as your Mouse 1 nodes, but coordinates are in **mesh local coordinates** (the coordinate system the OBJ/PLY uses). Format can match your `[NODES]` block:

```txt
[NODES]
head  ...
nose_tip ...
left_ear_tip ...
...
tail_root ...
tail_tip ...
left_front_paw ...
...
```

(Edges aren’t needed for rendering; only the node labels + positions.)

### 2) `cameras.json`
Store intrinsics + extrinsics per camera. For example:

```json
{
  "cameras": [
    {
      "name": "cam1_top",
      "width": 960,
      "height": 720,
      "fx": 800.0,
      "fy": 800.0,
      "cx": 480.0,
      "cy": 360.0,
      "R": [[...],[...],[...]],
      "t": [...]
    },
    ...
  ]
}
```

This makes the renderer independent of your simulator defaults. (You can also export this file from `mouse-sim.py` so both programs stay in sync.)

---

## Python stack that works well for this

### Mesh loading
- **`trimesh`**: reliably loads OBJ+MTL+textures (via PIL), and PLY.

### Rendering
Two common paths:

**Path A (pure Python, offscreen OpenGL):**
- **`pyrender`** + `trimesh`  
Pros: compact pipeline; handles textured meshes; gives you RGB images per frame.  
Cons: offscreen OpenGL can be annoying on some machines (needs a working GL context; headless sometimes needs EGL/OSMesa).

**Path B (Blender as renderer):**
- Python script generates per-frame transforms and calls **Blender** in background mode to render.  
Pros: most robust “realistic” output, best material/texture support.  
Cons: external dependency + heavier.

Given your goal (“much more realistic videos”), Blender is often the least painful long-term if you want lighting/shadows/materials to look good. If you mainly want “textured mouse mesh”, `pyrender` is fine.

---

## Core algorithm for frame-wise rigid alignment (Kabsch / Procrustes)
Let:
- `X` = Nx3 points from `mouse_mesh_nodes.txt` (mesh coordinate skeleton), using only chosen fit-nodes.
- `Y` = Nx3 points from `coords_3d.csv` at frame `k` (world coordinates), same labels.

Compute `R, t` minimizing `|| (R X + t) - Y ||` (least squares, rigid). This is the standard Kabsch algorithm.

Then transform the mesh vertices:
- `V_world = R V_mesh + t`

Render from each camera (intrinsics + extrinsics).

---

## Minimal “renderer” skeleton (pyrender + trimesh)
This is not a full finished program, but it shows the exact structure and the parts that matter:

```python
import json, csv
import numpy as np
import trimesh
import pyrender

def load_nodes_txt(path):
    nodes = {}
    mode = None
    with open(path, "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        for raw in f:
            line = raw.strip()
            if not line or line.startswith("#"):
                continue
            if line.upper() == "[NODES]":
                mode = "nodes"; continue
            if line.upper() == "[EDGES]":
                break
            if mode == "nodes":
                name, x, y, z = line.split()
                nodes[name.lower()] = np.array([float(x), float(y), float(z)], dtype=np.float64)
    return nodes

def kabsch(X, Y):
    # X, Y: (N,3)
    x0 = X.mean(axis=0)
    y0 = Y.mean(axis=0)
    Xc = X - x0
    Yc = Y - y0
    H = Xc.T @ Yc
    U, S, Vt = np.linalg.svd(H)
    R = Vt.T @ U.T
    if np.linalg.det(R) < 0:
        Vt[-1, :] *= -1
        R = Vt.T @ U.T
    t = y0 - R @ x0
    return R, t

def read_frame_points(coords_3d_csv, frame_idx):
    # returns dict label->(3,)
    pts = {}
    with open(coords_3d_csv, newline="", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        r = csv.DictReader(f)
        for row in r:
            if int(row["frame"]) != frame_idx:
                continue
            pts[row["node"].lower()] = np.array([float(row["x"]), float(row["y"]), float(row["z"])], dtype=np.float64)
    return pts

def build_camera(cam):
    # cam: dict with fx, fy, cx, cy, width, height, R, t
    fx, fy, cx, cy = cam["fx"], cam["fy"], cam["cx"], cam["cy"]
    w, h = cam["width"], cam["height"]
    K = np.array([[fx, 0, cx],
                  [0, fy, cy],
                  [0,  0,  1]], dtype=np.float64)
    pyr_cam = pyrender.IntrinsicsCamera(fx, fy, cx, cy, znear=1e-3, zfar=1e6)
    # pyrender camera pose is cam->world. You currently store world->cam as R,t.
    R_wc = np.array(cam["R"], dtype=np.float64)
    t_wc = np.array(cam["t"], dtype=np.float64)
    # world->cam: Pc = R_wc Pw + t_wc
    # invert to get cam->world pose:
    R_cw = R_wc.T
    t_cw = -R_wc.T @ t_wc
    pose = np.eye(4)
    pose[:3,:3] = R_cw
    pose[:3, 3] = t_cw
    return pyr_cam, pose, (w, h)

def main(mesh_path, coords_csv, mesh_nodes_txt, cameras_json, out_dir, fps=10):
    mesh = trimesh.load(mesh_path, force="mesh")  # textured OBJ should come with visuals
    pr_mesh = pyrender.Mesh.from_trimesh(mesh, smooth=True)

    with open(cameras_json, "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        cams = json.load(f)["cameras"]

    # choose fit nodes (exclude paws)
    mesh_nodes = load_nodes_txt(mesh_nodes_txt)
    fit_labels = [k for k in mesh_nodes.keys() if "paw" not in k]

    # set up per-camera scenes/renderers once
    renderers = []
    scenes = []
    cam_nodes = []
    sizes = []
    for cam in cams:
        scene = pyrender.Scene(bg_color=[0,0,0], ambient_light=[0.2,0.2,0.2])
        scene.add(pr_mesh, pose=np.eye(4))
        pyr_cam, cam_pose, (w,h) = build_camera(cam)
        cn = scene.add(pyr_cam, pose=cam_pose)
        # add a light so texture is visible
        light = pyrender.DirectionalLight(color=np.ones(3), intensity=3.0)
        scene.add(light, pose=cam_pose)
        scenes.append(scene)
        cam_nodes.append(cn)
        sizes.append((w,h))
        renderers.append(pyrender.OffscreenRenderer(w, h))

    # TODO: read number of frames from coords_csv efficiently (index it once)
    # For brevity: assume frames 0..N-1
    N = 600

    # TODO: write videos with cv2.VideoWriter or imageio
    for frame in range(N):
        sim_pts = read_frame_points(coords_csv, frame)
        X = np.stack([mesh_nodes[l] for l in fit_labels], axis=0)
        Y = np.stack([sim_pts[l] for l in fit_labels], axis=0)
        R, t = kabsch(X, Y)

        pose = np.eye(4)
        pose[:3,:3] = R
        pose[:3, 3] = t

        for scene, renderer in zip(scenes, renderers):
            # update mesh node pose
            # (pyrender stores nodes internally; simplest is remove/add or keep a handle to the mesh node)
            # For production code: keep the mesh node handle and set its matrix.
            scene.set_pose(scene.mesh_nodes[0], pose=pose)
            color, depth = renderer.render(scene)
            # write color frame to video

    for r in renderers:
        r.delete()
```

That’s the shape of it.

**Two important implementation details for a production version:**
- Don’t scan the entire `coords_3d.csv` for every frame (too slow). Build an index once (e.g., load into memory, or split to per-frame blocks).
- Keep handles to the mesh node in the scene to update its pose efficiently.

Add an option like `--export-cameras cameras.json` that writes out the actual cameras used. Then the renderer consumes that JSON.

User: Fur is absolutely unnecessary. 

But the transform from obj/mesh model coordinate system to cage coordinate system should not be rigid. What I can imagine is a (kind of) rubber sheet transform where the key/body part positions are matched precisely and inside the tetrahedrons (Delauny-like?) spanning the key points some interpolation is done, and outside the convex hull but inside the meshed object some kind of extrapolation w.r.t. to closest point inside the volume happens. Anyways a reasonable deformable transformation maintaining (largely) exact key point correspondence should suffice.

I am aware that this is compute intensive which is why all of this should not be inside mouse-sim 1 but rather be a stand-alone subsequent program that reads output generated by mouse 1 (c.f. my previous description).

Assistant: Yes — and separating it from `mouse-sim.py` is the right call.

What you’re describing is a **sparse constrained deformation** problem: you have a mesh in its own rest coordinate system, and for each frame you have target positions for a small set of labeled keypoints. You want a deformation field that:

- matches keypoints **exactly** (or nearly),
- interpolates “reasonably” through the interior,
- extrapolates “reasonably” outside the convex hull of the keypoints but still within the mouse volume,
- is fast enough to run per frame and render three videos.

With only ~10 keypoints, **Delaunay tetrahedra directly on those points will be too coarse and often degenerate** (and you’ll have large “unsupported” regions). You can still do a tetra-based method, but you’ll need **extra control points** or a **cage/tet mesh**.

Below are three viable approaches, ordered by practicality.

---

## 1) Recommended first implementation: rigid + RBF displacement field (simple, robust, exact keypoints)

### Idea
Per frame:

1) Estimate a **global rigid transform** (Kabsch) from a rigid subset of keypoints (e.g., head/ears/nose/tail_root/tail_tip; exclude paws if they’re “artificial”).
2) Apply it to the whole mesh ⇒ you’re already “mostly aligned”.
3) Compute residuals at all keypoints:  
   \[
   d_i = y_i - (R x_i + t)
   \]
4) Fit a smooth displacement field \( f(p) \) such that \( f(x_i)=d_i \) **exactly** using a radial basis interpolator (thin-plate / polyharmonic / cubic).
5) Deform all vertices:
   \[
   v' = (R v + t) + f(R v + t)
   \]

### Why this fits your spec
- **Exact correspondence**: choose an interpolatory RBF (smoothing=0).
- **Interior interpolation**: smooth field.
- **Outside convex hull**: RBF naturally extrapolates smoothly.
- **Compute cost**: with ~10–30 controls, evaluating \(f\) for 50k–200k vertices per frame is feasible with vectorized NumPy.

### One crucial addition: “anchor points” to prevent global mush
RBF extrapolation can do weird global warps unless you anchor it.

Add a set of extra control points around the mesh (e.g. 8 corners of the mesh bounding box, expanded by 10–20%), each with **zero residual displacement**. This makes the deformation decay and keeps the body from drifting like jelly.

### Minimal dependencies
- `trimesh` (load OBJ/PLY)
- `numpy`
- `scipy` (for RBF and/or solving)
- renderer: `pyrender` (OpenGL) **or** Blender CLI rendering

### RBF implementation choice
In SciPy, `scipy.interpolate.RBFInterpolator` works well for 3D displacement fields.

---

## 2) Piecewise linear tetrahedral deformation (what you sketched), but you’ll need a cage/tet mesh

### Idea
Precompute a tetrahedralization of a set of control points \(C\) that fill the mouse volume (not just the 10 keypoints). Then for each vertex:
- find the tetrahedron containing it,
- store barycentric coordinates,
- per frame, move tet vertices to their targets and reconstruct vertex by barycentric interpolation (affine in each tet).

### Pros
- Very fast per frame once precomputed.
- Exact at control points (tet vertices).
- No global wobbly extrapolation *if* the cage encloses the mesh.

### Cons
- **You need many more control points** than 10, otherwise most vertices won’t lie in any tetra that makes sense.
- Building a volumetric tet mesh around an organic body robustly is nontrivial in pure Python (usually you use TetGen / CGAL / Gmsh).

### Practical compromise
Use your 10 labeled keypoints + automatically generated auxiliary points:
- along the spine (interpolate head–tail_root),
- around cross-sections (offset in ±y, ±z),
- and bounding-box anchors.

Then tetrahedralize those controls.

If you want “first something that works”, RBF is easier.

---

## 3) As-Rigid-As-Possible (ARAP) / Laplacian deformation (best shape preservation, more work)

### Idea
Solve per frame for vertex positions minimizing a deformation energy (Laplacian / ARAP) with hard constraints at keypoints.

### Pros
- Looks “elastic but not rubbery”: preserves local shape, avoids volume collapse.
- Exact constraints possible.
- Pre-factorizable sparse systems ⇒ fast per frame after setup.

### Cons
- More code and numerical plumbing.
- ARAP often needs iterative local rotations.

If you later want “deformable but anatomical-ish”, this is the upgrade path.

---

# What I would implement next for you (Mouse 1 → realistic video)

## Program: `mouse_deform_render.py` (standalone)

### Inputs
- `--mesh mouse.obj` (or `.ply`)
- `--mesh-nodes mouse_mesh_nodes.txt` (same labels, mesh coordinates)
- `--sim-coords coords_3d.csv` (Mouse 1 output)
- `--cameras cameras.json` (exported from mouse-sim or hand-authored)
- `--out-dir out_render`
- options:
  - `--fit-nodes head,nose_tip,left_ear_tip,right_ear_tip,tail_root,tail_tip` (exclude paws by default)
  - `--method rbf|tet|arap` (start with `rbf`)
  - `--rbf-kernel thin_plate_spline` (or `cubic`)
  - `--rbf-anchors 8` (bbox corners)
  - `--decimate 0.5` (optional mesh simplification for speed)
  - `--fps 10`

### Per frame pipeline (RBF method)
1) Read keypoints for this frame.
2) Compute rigid `R,t` on fit-nodes (exclude paws).
3) Build control set:
   - all labeled nodes (or subset)
   - + anchor points with zero residuals
4) Fit RBF for residual displacement field.
5) Deform vertices.
6) Render from each camera → write 3 mp4 frames.

### Rendering choice
- If you want “just textured mesh in consistent lighting”: `pyrender` is fine.
- If you want “nice lighting/shadows/material fidelity” and maximum robustness: call Blender in background mode per frame (heavier but dependable visually).

---

## Important note about your current Mouse 1 outputs
Your running gait currently drives paws in a simplified way. If you include paws as hard constraints in the deformation, you’ll *force* the mesh to stretch to satisfy those positions. That’s why the two-stage approach matters:

- rigid fit using torso/head nodes,
- then deform with constraints that you *trust* (possibly excluding paws, or giving paws weaker influence if you choose a regularized method).

With RBF you can choose either:
- **hard constraints** (exact) on all keypoints, *or*
- hard constraints on torso/head, and treat paws as “soft” by using a tiny smoothing parameter or downweighting (SciPy’s RBFInterpolator supports smoothing, but not per-point weights; if you need weights, we implement the linear system ourselves).

---

## Next step: pick the deformation method and renderer
If you say “RBF + pyrender”, I’ll give you a complete runnable program (not pseudo-code) plus a conda `environment.yml` for it, and a small patch to `mouse-sim.py` to export `cameras.json`.

If you say “Blender renderer”, I’ll give you:
- a Python script that writes per-frame transforms/deformed meshes (or per-frame keypoint constraints),
- a Blender Python script that loads the OBJ once and renders all frames from the given cameras.

Both satisfy your “standalone subsequent program” requirement.

User: I prefer RBF+pyrender with exact constraints on all keypoints. Please go ahead coding the complete runnable program and the patch to mouse-sim.py. 

Can we combine the new environment.yml with the existing one for mouse-sim.py? Then both programs could run inside the same conda environment.

Assistant: Yes — we can do this cleanly as a **standalone program** that:

- loads your mesh (`.obj`/`.ply`, textures optional),
- reads `coords_3d.csv` (Mouse 1 output),
- reads `mouse_mesh_nodes.txt` (keypoints in the mesh’s coordinate system),
- reads `cameras.json` (exported from `mouse-sim.py`),
- for each frame computes:
  1) a **rigid base transform** (Kabsch) from mesh keypoints → simulated keypoints (using a *subset* for stability, configurable),
  2) an **RBF residual displacement field** in world space that enforces **exact constraints on all keypoints**,
  3) deforms all vertices and renders 3 MP4s with `pyrender`.

Below are:
1) the combined `environment.yml`,
2) the patch to `mouse-sim.py` to export `cameras.json`,
3) a complete runnable `mouse_deform_render.py`.

---

## 1) Combined conda environment.yml (runs both programs)

```yaml
name: mouse-sim
channels:
  - conda-forge
dependencies:
  - python=3.11
  - numpy
  - scipy
  - pillow
  - tqdm

  # Mouse 1 (videos + fallback)
  - opencv
  - imageio
  - imageio-ffmpeg

  # Mesh IO + rendering
  - trimesh
  - pyrender
  - pyglet
  - pyopengl
```

Create/activate:
```bash
conda env create -f environment.yml
conda activate mouse-sim
```

Note: `pyrender` offscreen rendering needs a working OpenGL context. On Linux headless you may need:
```bash
export PYOPENGL_PLATFORM=egl
# or: osmesa
```

## 2) Patch to `mouse-sim.py`: export cameras.json

### a) Add imports near the top
```python
import json
```

### b) Add a CLI flag in `build_argparser()`
Add near camera/video args:
```python
p.add_argument("--export-cameras", action="store_true",
               help="Write cameras.json (intrinsics + extrinsics) to out-dir.")
```

### c) Add a helper function (anywhere above simulate)
```python
def export_cameras_json(cams, path: str) -> None:
    data = {"cameras": []}
    for cam in cams:
        data["cameras"].append({
            "name": cam.name,
            "width": cam.width,
            "height": cam.height,
            "fx": float(cam.fx),
            "fy": float(cam.fy),
            "cx": float(cam.cx),
            "cy": float(cam.cy),
            # world -> camera: Pc = R*Pw + t
            "R": cam.R.tolist(),
            "t": cam.t.tolist(),
            "pos_world": cam.pos_world.tolist(),
        })
    with open(path, "w", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        json.dump(data, f, indent=2)
```

### d) Call it inside `simulate()` after cameras are created and `out_dir` exists
Right after:
```python
cams = make_default_cameras(...)
```
add:
```python
if args.export_cameras:
    cam_path = os.path.join(args.out_dir, "cameras.json")
    export_cameras_json(cams, cam_path)
```

Run Mouse 1 as usual, but add `--export-cameras`:
```bash
python mouse_sim.py --mouse-graph mouse2.txt --write-video --duration 60 --fps 10 --seed 2 --out-dir out2 --export-cameras
```

This will create `out2/cameras.json`.

## 3) Standalone deform+render program: `mouse_deform_render.py`

Save as `mouse_deform_render.py`.

```python
#!/usr/bin/env python3
from __future__ import annotations

import argparse
import csv
import json
import os
from dataclasses import dataclass
from typing import Dict, List, Tuple

import numpy as np
from scipy.interpolate import RBFInterpolator

import trimesh
import pyrender

try:
    import cv2  # type: ignore
except Exception as e:
    raise RuntimeError("OpenCV is required for writing MP4. Install opencv from conda-forge.") from e


def norm_label(s: str) -> str:
    return s.strip().lower().replace(" ", "_")


# ----------------------------
# IO: mesh keypoints
# ----------------------------
def load_nodes_txt(path: str) -> Dict[str, np.ndarray]:
    """
    Reads:
      [NODES]
      head  x y z
      ...

    Ignores [EDGES] and anything after.
    """
    if not os.path.exists(path):
        raise FileNotFoundError(path)

    nodes: Dict[str, np.ndarray] = {}
    mode = None
    with open(path, "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        for raw in f:
            line = raw.strip()
            if not line or line.startswith("#"):
                continue
            if line.upper() == "[NODES]":
                mode = "nodes"
                continue
            if line.upper() == "[EDGES]":
                break
            if mode == "nodes":
                parts = line.split()
                if len(parts) != 4:
                    raise ValueError(f"Bad node line: {line}")
                name = norm_label(parts[0])
                x, y, z = map(float, parts[1:])
                nodes[name] = np.array([x, y, z], dtype=np.float64)

    if not nodes:
        raise ValueError(f"No nodes found in {path}")
    return nodes


# ----------------------------
# IO: Mouse 1 coords_3d.csv (frame-indexed)
# ----------------------------
@dataclass
class FrameData:
    time: float
    behavior: int
    pts: Dict[str, np.ndarray]  # label -> (3,)


def load_coords_3d_csv(path: str) -> Dict[int, FrameData]:
    if not os.path.exists(path):
        raise FileNotFoundError(path)

    frames: Dict[int, FrameData] = {}
    with open(path, newline="", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        r = csv.DictReader(f)
        required = {"frame", "time", "behavior", "node", "x", "y", "z"}
        if not required.issubset(set(r.fieldnames or [])):
            raise ValueError(f"{path} missing required columns. Have: {r.fieldnames}")

        for row in r:
            fi = int(row["frame"])
            t = float(row["time"])
            b = int(row["behavior"])
            node = norm_label(row["node"])
            p = np.array([float(row["x"]), float(row["y"]), float(row["z"])], dtype=np.float64)

            if fi not in frames:
                frames[fi] = FrameData(time=t, behavior=b, pts={})
            # trust first time/behavior, but keep consistent
            frames[fi].pts[node] = p

    if not frames:
        raise ValueError(f"No frames loaded from {path}")
    return frames


# ----------------------------
# IO: cameras.json from mouse-sim
# ----------------------------
@dataclass
class CameraSpec:
    name: str
    width: int
    height: int
    fx: float
    fy: float
    cx: float
    cy: float
    R_wc: np.ndarray  # world -> cam
    t_wc: np.ndarray  # world -> cam


def load_cameras_json(path: str) -> List[CameraSpec]:
    if not os.path.exists(path):
        raise FileNotFoundError(path)

    with open(path, "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        data = json.load(f)
    cams = []
    for c in data["cameras"]:
        cams.append(CameraSpec(
            name=c["name"],
            width=int(c["width"]),
            height=int(c["height"]),
            fx=float(c["fx"]),
            fy=float(c["fy"]),
            cx=float(c["cx"]),
            cy=float(c["cy"]),
            R_wc=np.array(c["R"], dtype=np.float64),
            t_wc=np.array(c["t"], dtype=np.float64),
        ))
    if not cams:
        raise ValueError("No cameras in cameras.json")
    return cams


def world_to_cam_to_pose(R_wc: np.ndarray, t_wc: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    pyrender expects camera pose as cam->world 4x4.
    If world->cam is Pc = R_wc*Pw + t_wc, then:
      R_cw = R_wc^T
      t_cw = -R_wc^T * t_wc
    """
    R_cw = R_wc.T
    t_cw = -R_wc.T @ t_wc
    pose = np.eye(4, dtype=np.float64)
    pose[:3, :3] = R_cw
    pose[:3, 3] = t_cw
    return pose


# ----------------------------
# Math: Kabsch rigid alignment
# ----------------------------
def kabsch(X: np.ndarray, Y: np.ndarray) -> Tuple[np.ndarray, np.ndarray]:
    """
    Finds R,t minimizing ||R X + t - Y|| (least squares), with det(R)=+1
    X, Y: (N,3)
    Returns R(3,3), t(3,)
    """
    if X.shape != Y.shape or X.shape[1] != 3:
        raise ValueError("X and Y must be (N,3) and same shape")

    x0 = X.mean(axis=0)
    y0 = Y.mean(axis=0)
    Xc = X - x0
    Yc = Y - y0
    H = Xc.T @ Yc
    U, _, Vt = np.linalg.svd(H)
    R = Vt.T @ U.T
    if np.linalg.det(R) < 0:
        Vt[-1, :] *= -1
        R = Vt.T @ U.T
    t = y0 - R @ x0
    return R, t


# ----------------------------
# Mesh loading (OBJ/PLY)
# ----------------------------
def load_meshes(mesh_path: str) -> List[trimesh.Trimesh]:
    """
    Returns a list of trimesh.Trimesh objects.
    Supports OBJ (with MTL+textures) and PLY.
    """
    if not os.path.exists(mesh_path):
        raise FileNotFoundError(mesh_path)

    loaded = trimesh.load(mesh_path, force=None, process=False)

    meshes: List[trimesh.Trimesh] = []

    if isinstance(loaded, trimesh.Trimesh):
        meshes = [loaded]
    elif isinstance(loaded, trimesh.Scene):
        # Extract geometry. Keep each submesh (better chance to preserve visuals).
        for geom in loaded.geometry.values():
            if isinstance(geom, trimesh.Trimesh):
                meshes.append(geom)
    else:
        raise ValueError(f"Unsupported mesh load result type: {type(loaded)}")

    if not meshes:
        raise ValueError(f"No meshes extracted from {mesh_path}")

    # sanity
    for m in meshes:
        if m.vertices is None or len(m.vertices) == 0 or m.faces is None or len(m.faces) == 0:
            raise ValueError("Loaded a mesh with no vertices/faces.")
    return meshes


def mesh_bbox_anchors(meshes: List[trimesh.Trimesh], scale: float = 1.2) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    Returns 8 bbox corners in mesh space, expanded by 'scale' around center.
    Used as residual anchors with zero displacement to stabilize RBF extrapolation.
    """
    all_bounds = np.array([m.bounds for m in meshes], dtype=np.float64)  # (K,2,3)
    bmin = all_bounds[:, 0, :].min(axis=0)
    bmax = all_bounds[:, 1, :].max(axis=0)
    c = 0.5 * (bmin + bmax)
    e = 0.5 * (bmax - bmin) * scale

    corners = []
    for sx in (-1.0, 1.0):
        for sy in (-1.0, 1.0):
            for sz in (-1.0, 1.0):
                corners.append(c + np.array([sx, sy, sz]) * e)
    return np.stack(corners, axis=0)  # (8,3)


# ----------------------------
# Deformation: rigid base + exact RBF residual
# ----------------------------
def deform_vertices_rigid_rbf(
    V_mesh: np.ndarray,                 # (Nv,3) mesh vertices
    Xk_mesh: np.ndarray,                # (Nk,3) keypoints in mesh coords (ALL constraints)
    Yk_world: np.ndarray,               # (Nk,3) target keypoints in world (frame)
    Xfit_mesh: np.ndarray,              # (Nf,3) fit subset in mesh coords
    Yfit_world: np.ndarray,             # (Nf,3) fit subset in world coords
    anchors_mesh: np.ndarray | None,    # (Na,3) in mesh coords
    kernel: str,
) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    Returns V_world_deformed (Nv,3).
    Enforces exact constraints at ALL keypoints via interpolatory RBF on residuals.
    """
    # rigid base (for numerical stability + nice global motion)
    R, t = kabsch(Xfit_mesh, Yfit_world)

    V_base = (V_mesh @ R.T) + t  # (Nv,3)
    Xk_base = (Xk_mesh @ R.T) + t
    residuals = Yk_world - Xk_base

    if anchors_mesh is not None and len(anchors_mesh) > 0:
        A_base = (anchors_mesh @ R.T) + t
        P = np.vstack([Xk_base, A_base])
        D = np.vstack([residuals, np.zeros((A_base.shape[0], 3), dtype=np.float64)])
    else:
        P = Xk_base
        D = residuals

    # Exact interpolation: smoothing=0
    rbf = RBFInterpolator(P, D, kernel=kernel, smoothing=0.0)

    V_def = V_base + rbf(V_base)
    return V_def


# ----------------------------
# Rendering
# ----------------------------
def make_scene_for_camera(cam: CameraSpec) -> Tuple[pyrender.Scene, pyrender.OffscreenRenderer]:
    scene = pyrender.Scene(bg_color=[0, 0, 0], ambient_light=[0.25, 0.25, 0.25])

    pyr_cam = pyrender.IntrinsicsCamera(cam.fx, cam.fy, cam.cx, cam.cy, znear=1e-3, zfar=1e6)
    cam_pose = world_to_cam_to_pose(cam.R_wc, cam.t_wc)
    scene.add(pyr_cam, pose=cam_pose)

    # lighting: directional + point tied to camera pose (so object is always lit)
    dir_light = pyrender.DirectionalLight(color=np.ones(3), intensity=3.0)
    scene.add(dir_light, pose=cam_pose)

    point_light = pyrender.PointLight(color=np.ones(3), intensity=20.0)
    scene.add(point_light, pose=cam_pose)

    renderer = pyrender.OffscreenRenderer(viewport_width=cam.width, viewport_height=cam.height)
    return scene, renderer


def trimesh_to_pyrender(mesh: trimesh.Trimesh) -> pyrender.Mesh:
    # smooth=True gives nicer shading; textured visuals will pass through when supported
    return pyrender.Mesh.from_trimesh(mesh, smooth=True)


def main():
    ap = argparse.ArgumentParser(description="Deform textured mesh using Mouse 1 keypoints and render videos (RBF+pyrender).")
    ap.add_argument("--mesh", required=True, help="Path to mesh file (.obj or .ply). For OBJ, keep .mtl and textures alongside.")
    ap.add_argument("--mesh-nodes", required=True, help="mouse_mesh_nodes.txt: keypoint coords in mesh coordinate system ([NODES] section).")
    ap.add_argument("--coords-3d", required=True, help="coords_3d.csv output from mouse-sim.py.")
    ap.add_argument("--cameras", required=True, help="cameras.json exported by mouse-sim.py.")
    ap.add_argument("--out-dir", default="out_render", help="Output directory.")
    ap.add_argument("--fps", type=float, default=10.0, help="FPS for output videos.")

    ap.add_argument("--kernel", default="thin_plate_spline",
                    choices=["thin_plate_spline", "cubic", "quintic", "linear", "multiquadric", "inverse_multiquadric", "gaussian"],
                    help="RBF kernel.")
    ap.add_argument("--anchors", type=int, default=8,
                    help="Number of anchor points (0 disables). Currently: 8 means bbox corners.")
    ap.add_argument("--anchor-scale", type=float, default=1.25,
                    help="BBox anchor expansion scale around mesh.")
    ap.add_argument("--rigid-fit-nodes", default="head,nose_tip,left_ear_tip,right_ear_tip,tail_root,tail_tip",
                    help="Comma-separated labels for rigid base fit, or 'all'.")

    ap.add_argument("--start-frame", type=int, default=None)
    ap.add_argument("--end-frame", type=int, default=None)

    args = ap.parse_args()
    os.makedirs(args.out_dir, exist_ok=True)

    meshes = load_meshes(args.mesh)
    mesh_nodes = {norm_label(k): v for k, v in load_nodes_txt(args.mesh_nodes).items()}
    frames = load_coords_3d_csv(args.coords_3d)
    cams = load_cameras_json(args.cameras)

    # Determine frame range
    all_frame_ids = sorted(frames.keys())
    f0 = all_frame_ids[0] if args.start_frame is None else args.start_frame
    f1 = all_frame_ids[-1] if args.end_frame is None else args.end_frame
    frame_ids = [fi for fi in all_frame_ids if f0 <= fi <= f1]
    if not frame_ids:
        raise ValueError("No frames selected.")

    # Constraint labels: all nodes present in mesh_nodes (exact constraints)
    constraint_labels = sorted(mesh_nodes.keys())

    # Rigid fit labels
    if args.rigid_fit_nodes.strip().lower() == "all":
        fit_labels = constraint_labels
    else:
        fit_labels = [norm_label(s) for s in args.rigid_fit_nodes.split(",") if s.strip()]
    # validate
    for lab in constraint_labels:
        if lab not in mesh_nodes:
            raise ValueError(f"Missing in mesh_nodes: {lab}")
    for lab in fit_labels:
        if lab not in mesh_nodes:
            raise ValueError(f"Rigid-fit label not found in mesh_nodes: {lab}")

    # Anchors (mesh space)
    anchors_mesh = None
    if args.anchors != 0:
        # currently only supports 8 bbox anchors
        anchors_mesh = mesh_bbox_anchors(meshes, scale=args.anchor_scale)

    # Setup renderers + video writers
    scenes = {}
    renderers = {}
    writers = {}

    fourcc = cv2.VideoWriter_fourcc(*"mp4v")
    for cam in cams:
        scene, renderer = make_scene_for_camera(cam)
        scenes[cam.name] = scene
        renderers[cam.name] = renderer
        out_path = os.path.join(args.out_dir, f"{cam.name}.mp4")
        writers[cam.name] = cv2.VideoWriter(out_path, fourcc, args.fps, (cam.width, cam.height))

    # Pre-pack mesh keypoints arrays (mesh space)
    Xk_mesh = np.stack([mesh_nodes[l] for l in constraint_labels], axis=0)
    Xfit_mesh = np.stack([mesh_nodes[l] for l in fit_labels], axis=0)

    # Render loop
    for idx, fi in enumerate(frame_ids):
        fr = frames[fi]

        # Build target arrays in world space (must exist for each constraint label)
        missing = [l for l in constraint_labels if l not in fr.pts]
        if missing:
            raise ValueError(f"Frame {fi} missing keypoints: {missing}")

        Yk_world = np.stack([fr.pts[l] for l in constraint_labels], axis=0)
        Yfit_world = np.stack([fr.pts[l] for l in fit_labels], axis=0)

        # Deform each mesh geometry independently (preserving visuals)
        deformed_trimeshes: List[trimesh.Trimesh] = []
        for m in meshes:
            V_def = deform_vertices_rigid_rbf(
                V_mesh=np.asarray(m.vertices, dtype=np.float64),
                Xk_mesh=Xk_mesh,
                Yk_world=Yk_world,
                Xfit_mesh=Xfit_mesh,
                Yfit_world=Yfit_world,
                anchors_mesh=anchors_mesh,
                kernel=args.kernel,
            )
            # Keep faces + visuals; swap vertices
            dm = trimesh.Trimesh(
                vertices=V_def,
                faces=m.faces,
                visual=m.visual,
                process=False,
                maintain_order=True,
            )
            deformed_trimeshes.append(dm)

        # For each camera: add mesh nodes, render, remove, write frame
        for cam in cams:
            scene = scenes[cam.name]
            renderer = renderers[cam.name]

            # add all submeshes
            node_handles = []
            for dm in deformed_trimeshes:
                pr_mesh = trimesh_to_pyrender(dm)
                nh = scene.add(pr_mesh, pose=np.eye(4))
                node_handles.append(nh)

            color, _depth = renderer.render(scene)
            # pyrender gives RGB; cv2 wants BGR
            bgr = color[..., ::-1].copy()
            writers[cam.name].write(bgr)

            # remove mesh nodes
            for nh in node_handles:
                scene.remove_node(nh)

        if (idx + 1) % 50 == 0 or (idx + 1) == len(frame_ids):
            print(f"Rendered {idx+1}/{len(frame_ids)} frames")

    # cleanup
    for cam in cams:
        writers[cam.name].release()
        renderers[cam.name].delete()

    print("Done. Videos in:", args.out_dir)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

### Expected inputs
- `coords_3d.csv` from Mouse 1 (already produced)
- `cameras.json` from Mouse 1 (after adding `--export-cameras`)
- `mouse_mesh_nodes.txt` with `[NODES]` keypoints in **mesh coordinate system**, same labels as Mouse 1 uses (normalized names like `left_front_paw`, etc.)

Example `mouse_mesh_nodes.txt`:
```txt
[NODES]
head  ...
nose_tip ...
left_ear_tip ...
right_ear_tip ...
tail_root ...
tail_tip ...
left_front_paw ...
right_front_paw ...
left_hind_paw ...
right_hind_paw ...
```

---

## Example run (after you have out2 from Mouse 1)
```bash
python mouse_deform_render.py \
  --mesh mouse.obj \
  --mesh-nodes mouse_mesh_nodes.txt \
  --coords-3d out2/coords_3d.csv \
  --cameras out2/cameras.json \
  --out-dir out2_render \
  --fps 10 \
  --kernel thin_plate_spline \
  --rigid-fit-nodes head,nose_tip,left_ear_tip,right_ear_tip,tail_root,tail_tip
```

This will write:
- `out2_render/cam1_top.mp4`
- `out2_render/cam2_front.mp4`
- `out2_render/cam3_side.mp4`

---

## Two practical notes (so it behaves the way you intend)

1) **Exact constraints on all keypoints** can produce extreme warps if some keypoints in Mouse 1 are “procedural” (especially paws). Your choice is valid; just be aware it’s mathematically faithful to those points.

2) The `--rigid-fit-nodes` list matters a lot for stability.  
Even with exact RBF constraints on all nodes, I strongly recommend keeping the rigid fit on torso/head nodes (default) rather than “all”, because it prevents the base motion from being dragged around by paw artifacts.

If you want, I can also add an option to dump a single deformed mesh as `.ply` for any chosen frame (to inspect the deformation quality in Meshlab before rendering full videos).

User: So it does not need the .mtl and .tga?

Assistant: It **doesn’t require** `.mtl` and `.tga` to run — but it **can use them automatically** if you give it the `.obj` and the files are available.

### What happens by format

**If you load a `.ply`:**
- Geometry loads (vertices/faces).
- Usually no materials/textures.
- Render will be a plain shaded mesh (still “realistic” via lighting, just not textured).

**If you load a `.obj`:**
- Geometry loads.
- If the OBJ references an `.mtl` (`mtllib ...`) and that MTL references images (`map_Kd ...` etc.) and the image files exist (e.g. `.tga`), then `trimesh` will typically load those visuals and `pyrender` will render the texture.
- If the `.mtl` or image files are missing or paths don’t match, it will fall back to untextured shading.

### Practical rule
- If you want texture: keep `mouse.obj`, `mouse.mtl`, and `mouse_01.tga` in the **same directory**, and run with `--mesh mouse.obj`.  
- If you don’t care: you can use `--mesh mouse.ply` and ignore MTL/TGA entirely.

### One caveat
`.tga` support depends on `Pillow` (included in the environment I gave). If the texture still doesn’t show, it’s usually due to **MTL paths** (relative paths inside the `.mtl`). In that case, opening the `.mtl` and ensuring `map_Kd mouse_01.tga` (or correct relative path) fixes it.

If you want, paste the `mtl` contents (it’s small), and I’ll tell you exactly whether it points to the `.tga` correctly.

Assistant: It *can* use `.mtl` + `.tga` automatically — but **your current `.mtl` will not work as-is**, because it references the texture via an **absolute Windows path** that won’t exist on your machine:

- `map_Kd D:\WORK\...\mouse_01.tga`
- `map_Ka D:\WORK\...\mouse_01.tga` fileciteturn1file0

So in practice:

- **`.ply`**: works *without* `.mtl/.tga` (plain shaded mesh).
- **`.obj`**: will only show the texture if the `.mtl` points to a texture path that can be resolved.

## Fix (recommended): make the texture path relative
Put these files in the same folder:

- `mouse.obj`
- `mouse.mtl`
- `mouse_01.tga`

Then edit the `.mtl` so the texture lines become **relative filenames**, e.g.:

```mtl
map_Ka mouse_01.tga
map_Kd mouse_01.tga
```

That’s usually enough for `trimesh` → `pyrender` to pick up the texture.

### Also check the OBJ references the MTL correctly
Open `mouse.obj` and make sure it has a line like:

```obj
mtllib mouse.mtl
```

If the OBJ refers to a different material filename, either rename the `.mtl` to match, or update the `mtllib` line.

## If TGA causes trouble
Even with Pillow installed, some assets have odd TGA variants. If the texture still doesn’t render, convert `mouse_01.tga` → `mouse_01.png` and update:

```mtl
map_Kd mouse_01.png
```

## Bottom line
- **No, the renderer does not *need* `.mtl/.tga`** (PLY or untextured OBJ is fine).
- **Yes, it can use them** — but you must fix those absolute paths in the MTL (or convert + relink).

User: The .obj file is binary, isn't it. How can I open it to check whether it contains mtllib mouse.ml?

Assistant: Wavefront **`.obj` is normally plain text (ASCII)**, not binary. So in the usual case you can open it and you’ll literally see lines like:

```obj
mtllib mouse.mtl
usemtl MaterialName
v  ...
vt ...
vn ...
f  ...
```

If it *looks* “binary” when you open it, the common reasons are:

- it’s **very large** and your editor chokes / shows garbage
- it’s actually **compressed** (e.g. a `.gz` or `.zip` renamed to `.obj`)
- it’s not an OBJ at all (wrong extension)

### Fast ways to check on Linux/macOS
Run these in a terminal in the folder with the file:

```bash
file mouse.obj
head -n 20 mouse.obj
grep -n -i '^mtllib' mouse.obj
grep -n -i 'usemtl' mouse.obj
```

If `grep` complains about “binary file matches”, force it to treat it as text:

```bash
grep -a -n -i '^mtllib' mouse.obj
```

If the file is huge and `head` is slow, just search for the keyword:

```bash
strings mouse.obj | grep -i mtllib | head
```

### On Windows
- Use **VS Code** or **Notepad++** (they handle big text files better than Notepad).
- Or use PowerShell:

```powershell
Select-String -Path .\mouse.obj -Pattern "mtllib"
Select-String -Path .\mouse.obj -Pattern "usemtl"
```

### Check whether it’s actually compressed
Look at the first few bytes (“magic”):

```bash
xxd -l 16 mouse.obj
```

If you see:
- `50 4B` → it’s a ZIP (`PK`)
- `1F 8B` → it’s gzip
- `78 9C` → zlib

In that case, rename/decompress appropriately.

### If it’s truly text but missing `mtllib`
You can fix it by adding a line at the very top of the OBJ (in a text editor):

```obj
mtllib mouse.mtl
```

…and make sure `mouse.mtl` sits next to `mouse.obj` and that the `.mtl` uses a **relative** texture path (as discussed).

If you want, upload the `mouse.obj` (or just the first ~50 lines), and I’ll tell you immediately whether it references the MTL and what filename it expects.

In your `.mtl`, set:

…and keep the file exactly as it is.

This is the safest if you don’t want to touch filenames.

Rename the file on disk to `mouse_01.tga` and keep the `.mtl` as:

```mtl
map_Kd mouse_01.tga

```

This is convenient if you prefer “clean” names and want the asset folder to look standard.

For simplest resolution, ensure these live together:

- `mouse.obj`
- `mouse.mtl`
- your texture file (`.tga`)

and the `.mtl` uses just the filename (no path).

Assistant: `ModuleNotFoundError: No module named 'trimesh'` means **your currently activated conda env (`(mouse_sim)`) does not actually have `trimesh` installed**, even though the `environment.yml` *file* lists it.

That happens in two common cases:

- you **created the env before** you added `trimesh` to `environment.yml` (so the env is out of date), or
- you’re editing `environment.yml` in one folder but running an env created from a *different* yml / different env name.

### 1) First, confirm which env you’re in
```bash

python -c "import sys; print(sys.executable)"
```

If the env name shows as `mouse_sim` (underscore) but your YAML says `name: mouse-sim` (hyphen), then you likely have **two different environments**. That mismatch alone can explain it.

Assistant: Yes — and your simulation output is an unusually clean setup for benchmarking sequence models (because you can generate unlimited labeled data with known ground truth).

Below is a concrete, “you can build it this week” framework: dataset format, embeddings that work, and three comparable model baselines (**LSTM**, **xLSTM**, **Transformer encoder**) that all take the *same* per-frame feature vectors and output a class **per frame**.

I’ll also include an environment spec you can merge into your existing conda env.

---

## Data design

### Inputs
From `coords_3d.csv` (Mouse 1 output): per frame `t`, and per keypoint `x,y,z`, plus `behavior` (0/1). You already have this.

### How to make the model’s life easy
Raw cage/world coordinates bake in nuisance factors (translation, heading, scale). Normalize into a **body-centric coordinate system**:

Recommended per frame:
1) Choose a root, e.g. `tail_root` (or your “reference point” if you output it).
2) **Translate** all keypoints so root is at origin.
3) **Rotate around z** so the mouse forward axis points to +x:
   - forward axis can be `head - tail_root` projected to x–y.
4) Optionally **scale** by a size scalar (e.g. `||head-tail_root||` in rest pose) to reduce dependency on mesh units (cm/mm).

Then build features:
- positions: `(K,3)` flattened to `(3K,)`
- velocities: `Δpos = pos[t] - pos[t-1]` (also `(3K,)`)
- (optional) accelerations, or a few angles/distances if you want a strong classical baseline.

This normalization is the single biggest difference between “works immediately” and “why is this hard?”.

---

## Embedding: “coordinates as words”
Treat each **frame** as a token (that’s the most reliable first pass):

- Input per time step: `x_t ∈ R^(F)` where `F = 3K` or `F = 6K` (pos+vel).
- Embed with a small MLP/linear layer: `e_t = W x_t + b` to `d_model`.

This is the standard approach for time-series Transformers and RNNs; no need to quantize coordinates into discrete tokens.

---

## Models to compare

### 1) LSTM baseline
- `nn.LSTM(input_size=F, hidden_size=H, num_layers=L, bidirectional=True/False)`
- per frame logits via a linear head. citeturn0search3

### 2) xLSTM
Use the paper + official repo to avoid “random GitHub re-implementations” drift: citeturn0search0turn0search13  
You can wrap their module similarly: input `(B,T,F)` → embed → xLSTM → per-frame head.

### 3) Transformer encoder
- `nn.TransformerEncoder(TransformerEncoderLayer(...), num_layers=N)` with positional encoding. citeturn0search2turn0search6

Keep everything else identical (same preprocessing, same window length, same optimizer) so the comparison is meaningful.

---

## Training protocol that won’t fool you

- Generate many independent simulations (different seeds).
- Split by **run**, not by frame (avoid leakage).
- Train on sliding windows of length `T` (e.g. 20 frames = 2s at 10 fps).
- Predict per-frame class for the window and compute loss on all frames.
- Metrics: frame accuracy + F1 for rearing (class 1 is likely rarer).

---

## Minimal runnable framework (PyTorch)

I’m giving you a compact but complete skeleton with:
- CSV loader
- body-centric normalization
- windowing dataset
- 3 model classes (LSTM / Transformer / xLSTM stub)
- training loop

### `train_behavior.py`
```python
#!/usr/bin/env python3
from __future__ import annotations

import argparse
import csv
import math
import os
from dataclasses import dataclass
from typing import Dict, List, Tuple

import numpy as np
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.data import Dataset, DataLoader


# ----------------------------
# Data utilities
# ----------------------------

def nlabel(s: str) -> str:
    return s.strip().lower().replace(" ", "_")

@dataclass
class Run:
    # frames: list of times, (T,K,3) positions, (T,) labels
    times: np.ndarray
    pos: np.ndarray
    y: np.ndarray
    node_order: List[str]

def load_coords_3d_csv(path: str, node_order: List[str] | None = None) -> Run:
    """
    Expects coords_3d.csv with columns: frame,time,behavior,node,x,y,z
    Assumes single run in file. (You can extend to multiple runs by concatenation.)
    """
    rows = []
    with open(path, newline="", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        r = csv.DictReader(f)
        for row in r:
            rows.append(row)

    frames = sorted(set(int(r["frame"]) for r in rows))
    if not frames:
        raise ValueError("No frames found")

    # discover node_order if not given
    if node_order is None:
        node_order = sorted(set(nlabel(r["node"]) for r in rows))

    node_to_i = {n: i for i, n in enumerate(node_order)}
    T = len(frames)
    K = len(node_order)

    times = np.zeros((T,), dtype=np.float32)
    y = np.zeros((T,), dtype=np.int64)
    pos = np.zeros((T, K, 3), dtype=np.float32)

    # group rows by frame
    by_frame: Dict[int, List[dict]] = {f: [] for f in frames}
    for r in rows:
        by_frame[int(r["frame"])].append(r)

    for ti, fidx in enumerate(frames):
        fr = by_frame[fidx]
        # time/label: assume consistent within a frame
        times[ti] = float(fr[0]["time"])
        y[ti] = int(fr[0]["behavior"])
        for rr in fr:
            node = nlabel(rr["node"])
            if node not in node_to_i:
                continue
            j = node_to_i[node]
            pos[ti, j, 0] = float(rr["x"])
            pos[ti, j, 1] = float(rr["y"])
            pos[ti, j, 2] = float(rr["z"])

    return Run(times=times, pos=pos, y=y, node_order=node_order)

def body_frame_normalize(pos: np.ndarray, node_order: List[str],
                         root: str = "tail_root", head: str = "head",
                         eps: float = 1e-8) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    pos: (T,K,3) in world/cage coords.
    Output: (T,K,3) body-centric coords:
      - translate so root at origin
      - rotate around z so forward (root->head) points to +x
    """
    root = nlabel(root); head = nlabel(head)
    idx = {n: i for i, n in enumerate(node_order)}
    if root not in idx or head not in idx:
        raise ValueError("root/head not found in node_order")

    r = pos[:, idx[root], :]  # (T,3)
    h = pos[:, idx[head], :]  # (T,3)

    p = pos - r[:, None, :]   # translate

    fwd = h - r
    fwd_xy = fwd[:, :2]
    ang = np.arctan2(fwd_xy[:, 1], fwd_xy[:, 0])  # angle from +x
    # rotate by -ang so fwd aligns to +x
    ca = np.cos(-ang); sa = np.sin(-ang)

    out = p.copy()
    x = p[..., 0]; y = p[..., 1]
    out[..., 0] = ca[:, None] * x - sa[:, None] * y
    out[..., 1] = sa[:, None] * x + ca[:, None] * y
    # z unchanged
    return out

def make_features(pos_body: np.ndarray, use_vel: bool = True) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    pos_body: (T,K,3)
    returns X: (T,F)
    """
    T, K, _ = pos_body.shape
    p = pos_body.reshape(T, 3*K)
    if not use_vel:
        return p
    v = np.zeros_like(p)
    v[1:] = p[1:] - p[:-1]
    return np.concatenate([p, v], axis=1)

class WindowDataset(Dataset):
    def __init__(self, X: np.ndarray, y: np.ndarray, T: int, stride: int):
        self.X = torch.tensor(X, dtype=torch.float32)
        self.y = torch.tensor(y, dtype=torch.long)
        self.T = T
        self.stride = stride
        self.starts = list(range(0, len(X) - T + 1, stride))

    def __len__(self) -> int:
        return len(self.starts)

    def __getitem__(self, i: int):
        s = self.starts[i]
        return self.X[s:s+self.T], self.y[s:s+self.T]


# ----------------------------
# Models
# ----------------------------

class LSTMClassifier(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_dim: int, hidden: int = 128, layers: int = 2, bidir: bool = True, dropout: float = 0.1):
        super().__init__()
        self.lstm = nn.LSTM(
            input_size=in_dim,
            hidden_size=hidden,
            num_layers=layers,
            batch_first=True,
            dropout=dropout if layers > 1 else 0.0,
            bidirectional=bidir,
        )
        out_dim = hidden * (2 if bidir else 1)
        self.head = nn.Linear(out_dim, 2)

    def forward(self, x):  # (B,T,F)
        z, _ = self.lstm(x)
        return self.head(z)  # (B,T,2)

class PositionalEncoding(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, d_model: int, max_len: int = 4096):
        super().__init__()
        pe = torch.zeros(max_len, d_model)
        pos = torch.arange(0, max_len).float().unsqueeze(1)
        div = torch.exp(torch.arange(0, d_model, 2).float() * (-math.log(10000.0) / d_model))
        pe[:, 0::2] = torch.sin(pos * div)
        pe[:, 1::2] = torch.cos(pos * div)
        self.register_buffer("pe", pe.unsqueeze(0))  # (1,max_len,d_model)

    def forward(self, x):
        return x + self.pe[:, :x.size(1), :]

class TransformerClassifier(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_dim: int, d_model: int = 128, nhead: int = 4, layers: int = 4, ff: int = 256, dropout: float = 0.1):
        super().__init__()
        self.embed = nn.Linear(in_dim, d_model)
        self.pe = PositionalEncoding(d_model)
        enc_layer = nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=d_model, nhead=nhead, dim_feedforward=ff, dropout=dropout, batch_first=True)
        self.enc = nn.TransformerEncoder(enc_layer, num_layers=layers)
        self.head = nn.Linear(d_model, 2)

    def forward(self, x):
        z = self.embed(x)
        z = self.pe(z)
        z = self.enc(z)
        return self.head(z)

class xLSTMClassifier(nn.Module):
    """
    Thin wrapper; requires installing an xLSTM implementation.
    Prefer the official repo (NX-AI/xlstm). citeturn0search13
    """
    def __init__(self, in_dim: int, d_model: int = 128):
        super().__init__()
        self.embed = nn.Linear(in_dim, d_model)

        # Placeholder: wire in your chosen xLSTM module here.
        # Example (pseudo):
        #   from xlstm import xLSTM
        #   self.core = xLSTM(d_model, ...)
        self.core = nn.Identity()

        self.head = nn.Linear(d_model, 2)

    def forward(self, x):
        z = self.embed(x)
        z = self.core(z)
        return self.head(z)


# ----------------------------
# Training
# ----------------------------

def train_one(args):
    run = load_coords_3d_csv(args.coords_3d)
    pos_body = body_frame_normalize(run.pos, run.node_order, root=args.root, head=args.head)
    X = make_features(pos_body, use_vel=not args.no_vel)
    y = run.y

    # split by time index for this single-run example (for real use: split by run/seed)
    n = len(X)
    n_train = int(0.8 * n)
    Xtr, ytr = X[:n_train], y[:n_train]
    Xva, yva = X[n_train:], y[n_train:]

    ds_tr = WindowDataset(Xtr, ytr, T=args.window, stride=args.stride)
    ds_va = WindowDataset(Xva, yva, T=args.window, stride=args.stride)

    dl_tr = DataLoader(ds_tr, batch_size=args.batch, shuffle=True, num_workers=0, drop_last=True)
    dl_va = DataLoader(ds_va, batch_size=args.batch, shuffle=False, num_workers=0)

    in_dim = X.shape[1]
    if args.model == "lstm":
        model = LSTMClassifier(in_dim, hidden=args.hidden, layers=args.layers, bidir=not args.no_bidir, dropout=args.dropout)
    elif args.model == "transformer":
        model = TransformerClassifier(in_dim, d_model=args.d_model, nhead=args.nhead, layers=args.layers, ff=args.ff, dropout=args.dropout)
    elif args.model == "xlstm":
        model = xLSTMClassifier(in_dim, d_model=args.d_model)
    else:
        raise ValueError(args.model)

    device = torch.device("cuda" if torch.cuda.is_available() and not args.cpu else "cpu")
    model.to(device)

    opt = torch.optim.AdamW(model.parameters(), lr=args.lr, weight_decay=args.wd)
    crit = nn.CrossEntropyLoss()

    def eval_loop():
        model.eval()
        total, correct = 0, 0
        with torch.no_grad():
            for xb, yb in dl_va:
                xb = xb.to(device)
                yb = yb.to(device)
                logits = model(xb)  # (B,T,2)
                pred = logits.argmax(dim=-1)
                total += yb.numel()
                correct += (pred == yb).sum().item()
        return correct / max(1, total)

    for epoch in range(1, args.epochs + 1):
        model.train()
        for xb, yb in dl_tr:
            xb = xb.to(device)
            yb = yb.to(device)
            logits = model(xb)
            loss = crit(logits.view(-1, 2), yb.view(-1))
            opt.zero_grad()
            loss.backward()
            nn.utils.clip_grad_norm_(model.parameters(), args.clip)
            opt.step()

        acc = eval_loop()
        print(f"epoch {epoch:03d}  val_acc={acc:.4f}")

    # Save
    if args.save:
        torch.save(model.state_dict(), args.save)
        print("saved:", args.save)


def main():
    ap = argparse.ArgumentParser()
    ap.add_argument("--coords-3d", required=True)
    ap.add_argument("--model", choices=["lstm", "xlstm", "transformer"], default="lstm")
    ap.add_argument("--root", default="tail_root")
    ap.add_argument("--head", default="head")
    ap.add_argument("--no-vel", action="store_true")

    ap.add_argument("--window", type=int, default=20)
    ap.add_argument("--stride", type=int, default=1)
    ap.add_argument("--batch", type=int, default=64)
    ap.add_argument("--epochs", type=int, default=30)
    ap.add_argument("--lr", type=float, default=3e-4)
    ap.add_argument("--wd", type=float, default=1e-4)
    ap.add_argument("--clip", type=float, default=1.0)

    # shared-ish
    ap.add_argument("--dropout", type=float, default=0.1)
    ap.add_argument("--layers", type=int, default=2)
    ap.add_argument("--hidden", type=int, default=128)   # lstm
    ap.add_argument("--no-bidir", action="store_true")

    # transformer/xlstm
    ap.add_argument("--d-model", type=int, default=128)
    ap.add_argument("--nhead", type=int, default=4)
    ap.add_argument("--ff", type=int, default=256)

    ap.add_argument("--cpu", action="store_true")
    ap.add_argument("--save", default=None)
    args = ap.parse_args()
    train_one(args)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

## One warning that will matter later
When you transition to real keypoint detectors, you’ll get:
- noise,
- missing points,
- occasional swaps.

So after you get good accuracy on clean simulated data, the next step is **domain randomization**:
- add Gaussian noise to coords,
- randomly drop keypoints,
- small camera-frame jitter (if you train from 2D).

That keeps the model from overfitting to “perfect physics”.
