```python
        tex = {"T": args.T, "B": args.B, "N": args.N, "S": args.S, "E": args.E, "W": args.W}
        # compute skip_face as before
        Cw = camera_center_world(cam.R_wc, cam.t_wc)
        fw = camera_forward_world_cv(cam.R_wc)
        skip_face = pick_face_to_skip(Cw, fw, cage)
        add_cage_to_scene(scene_cage, cage, tex, alpha=args.cage_alpha, skip_face=skip_face)

        scene_cage.bg_color = [30, 30, 30]
        scene_cage.ambient_light = [0.8, 0.8, 0.8]

        # also brighten mouse scene so it matches
        scene_mouse.bg_color = [0, 0, 0]
        scene_mouse.ambient_light = [0.8, 0.8, 0.8]

    scenes_cage[cam.name] = scene_cage
    scenes_mouse[cam.name] = scene_mouse
    renderers[cam.name] = renderer
    ...
```

**Important:** Don’t actually keep the second renderer; you only need one per camera. If you want the clean version, I can show the small refactor so you don’t create a renderer twice.

### 3) In the frame loop: render cage, then render mouse-only, then composite
Where you currently do:

```python
color, _depth = renderer.render(scene)
```

Replace camera loop body with:

```python
scene_cage = scenes_cage[cam.name]
scene_mouse = scenes_mouse[cam.name]
renderer = renderers[cam.name]

# --- render cage pass (no mouse meshes in this scene)
cage_rgb, _ = renderer.render(scene_cage)

# --- render mouse pass (add mouse meshes only to scene_mouse)
node_handles = []
for dm in deformed_trimeshes:
    pr_mesh = trimesh_to_pyrender(dm)
    nh = scene_mouse.add(pr_mesh, pose=np.eye(4))
    node_handles.append(nh)

mouse_rgb, mouse_depth = renderer.render(scene_mouse)

for nh in node_handles:
    scene_mouse.remove_node(nh)

if args.mouse_on_top:
    # mouse_depth is 0 where nothing was drawn (background)
    mask = mouse_depth > 0
    out = cage_rgb.copy()
    out[mask] = mouse_rgb[mask]
else:
    # “physical” mode: render everything together (your current approach)
    # easiest: keep your existing single-scene path instead of this branch
    out = cage_rgb  # (but you'd want the combined scene render here)
```

Then write `out` to video as before (convert RGB→BGR).

### Result
- Cage looks correct and stays static.
- Mouse always appears “in front” because you overwrite pixels wherever mouse rendered.

---

## Notes / trade-offs
- This is intentionally non-physical: the mouse will be visible even when it “should” be behind a wall.
- It still looks nice for demos and for training pipelines where you don’t want occlusion artifacts.

## Quick alternative (even simpler)
If you don’t care about walls ever occluding anything, you can also just render with `RenderFlags.SKIP_DEPTH_TEST` *if available* — but pyrender’s public flags aren’t consistent across versions. The **depth-mask composite** above is stable.

Assistant: Yes — with your current `make_scene_for_camera`, the clean refactor is:

- **Create a renderer once per camera**
- Create **two scenes** per camera:
  - `scene_cage`: camera + lights + cage (no mouse)
  - `scene_mouse`: camera + lights only (mouse added/removed per frame)
- If `--mouse-on-top`: render both passes and **composite mouse over cage** using the mouse depth buffer.

Below is the minimal, consistent change set.

## 1) Replace `make_scene_for_camera` with two functions

```python
def make_scene_for_camera(cam: CameraSpec,
                          bg_color=(0, 0, 0),
                          ambient=(0.25, 0.25, 0.25)) -> pyrender.Scene:
    scene = pyrender.Scene(bg_color=list(bg_color), ambient_light=list(ambient))

    pyr_cam = pyrender.IntrinsicsCamera(cam.fx, cam.fy, cam.cx, cam.cy, znear=1e-3, zfar=1e6)
    cam_pose = world_to_cam_to_pose(cam.R_wc, cam.t_wc)
    scene.add(pyr_cam, pose=cam_pose)

    # lighting: directional + point tied to camera pose (so object is always lit)
    dir_light = pyrender.DirectionalLight(color=np.ones(3), intensity=3.0)
    scene.add(dir_light, pose=cam_pose)

    point_light = pyrender.PointLight(color=np.ones(3), intensity=20.0)
    scene.add(point_light, pose=cam_pose)

    return scene


def make_renderer_for_camera(cam: CameraSpec) -> pyrender.OffscreenRenderer:
    return pyrender.OffscreenRenderer(viewport_width=cam.width, viewport_height=cam.height)
```

## 2) Add CLI flag

```python

                help="Render mouse over cage regardless of depth (non-physical).")
```

## 3) Setup: create one renderer + two scenes per camera

Replace your setup loop with something like:

```python
scenes_cage = {}
scenes_mouse = {}
renderers = {}
writers = {}

fourcc = cv2.VideoWriter_fourcc(*"mp4v")

for cam in cams:
    # One renderer per camera
    renderer = make_renderer_for_camera(cam)

    # Two scenes sharing the same camera+lights configuration
    scene_cage = make_scene_for_camera(cam, bg_color=(30, 30, 30), ambient=(0.8, 0.8, 0.8))
    scene_mouse = make_scene_for_camera(cam, bg_color=(0, 0, 0), ambient=(0.8, 0.8, 0.8))

    if cage is not None:
        tex = {"T": args.T, "B": args.B, "N": args.N, "S": args.S, "E": args.E, "W": args.W}
        Cw = camera_center_world(cam.R_wc, cam.t_wc)
        fw = camera_forward_world_cv(cam.R_wc)
        skip_face = pick_face_to_skip(Cw, fw, cage)
        add_cage_to_scene(scene_cage, cage, tex, alpha=args.cage_alpha, skip_face=skip_face)

    scenes_cage[cam.name] = scene_cage
    scenes_mouse[cam.name] = scene_mouse
    renderers[cam.name] = renderer

    out_path = os.path.join(args.out_dir, f"{cam.name}.mp4")
    writers[cam.name] = cv2.VideoWriter(out_path, fourcc, args.fps, (cam.width, cam.height))
```

---

## 4) Frame loop: two-pass render + composite (only when requested)

Inside your per-camera render code in the main loop, replace the single render with:

```python
renderer = renderers[cam.name]

if args.mouse_on_top and cage is not None:
    # Pass 1: cage only (static scene)
    cage_rgb, _ = renderer.render(scenes_cage[cam.name])

    # Pass 2: mouse only
    scene_mouse = scenes_mouse[cam.name]
    node_handles = []
    for dm in deformed_trimeshes:
        pr_mesh = trimesh_to_pyrender(dm)
        nh = scene_mouse.add(pr_mesh, pose=np.eye(4))
        node_handles.append(nh)

    mouse_rgb, mouse_depth = renderer.render(scene_mouse)

    for nh in node_handles:
        scene_mouse.remove_node(nh)

    # Composite: wherever mouse rendered (depth > 0), overwrite cage pixels
    mask = mouse_depth > 0
    out_rgb = cage_rgb.copy()
    out_rgb[mask] = mouse_rgb[mask]

else:
    # Physical mode (recommended default): render mouse + cage together
    scene = scenes_cage[cam.name]
    node_handles = []
    for dm in deformed_trimeshes:
        pr_mesh = trimesh_to_pyrender(dm)
        nh = scene.add(pr_mesh, pose=np.eye(4))
        node_handles.append(nh)

    out_rgb, _ = renderer.render(scene)

    for nh in node_handles:
        scene.remove_node(nh)

# write frame
bgr = out_rgb[..., ::-1].copy()
writers[cam.name].write(bgr)
```

### Notes
- I gated the cheat mode on `cage is not None` so it’s meaningful. If you also want “mouse on top of black background”, just remove that condition.
- This guarantees the mouse is always in front of the wall textures, even if it would be behind them.

```python
if args.mouse_on_top and cage is not None:
    cage_rgb, _ = renderer.render(scene_cage)
    pre_cage_rgb[cam.name] = cage_rgb
```

Then in the frame loop use `cage_rgb = pre_cage_rgb[cam.name]`.


### Important setup change
This replacement assumes you already changed your setup dictionaries from:

- `scenes[cam.name] = scene`

to:

- `scenes_cage[cam.name] = scene_cage`
- `scenes_mouse[cam.name] = scene_mouse`

…and that `scene_cage` contains the cage faces, while `scene_mouse` does not.

---

## Small optional improvement (faster): pre-render cage once
Since the cage doesn’t move, you can compute `cage_rgb` once per camera during setup and reuse it in `--mouse-on-top` mode.

At setup time (after adding cage to `scene_cage`):

```python
pre_cage_rgb = {}
if args.mouse_on_top and cage is not None:
    pre_cage_rgb[cam.name], _ = renderer.render(scene_cage)
```

Then replace `cage_rgb, _ = renderer.render(...)` with:

```python
cage_rgb = pre_cage_rgb[cam.name]
```

Assistant: That `KeyError: 'cam1_top'` means exactly one thing:

**`pre_cage_rgb` was never filled for that camera name**, but later you try to read it.

So the bug is in the *setup phase*, not the frame loop.

Typical causes:
- `pre_cage_rgb = {}` is created **inside** the camera loop (so it gets reset each iteration).
- The code that pre-renders the cage is under a condition that didn’t run for `cam1_top` (or ran before the cage was added).
- You pre-rendered only some cameras by accident.

### Fix (robust): fill `pre_cage_rgb` for *every* camera, and also use a safe fallback

#### 1) Make sure `pre_cage_rgb` is defined **before** the camera loop
```python
pre_cage_rgb = {}
```

#### 2) In the *camera setup loop*, after the cage is added to `scene_cage`, pre-render and store it
```python
for cam in cams:
    renderer = make_renderer_for_camera(cam)

    scene_cage = make_scene_for_camera(cam, bg_color=(30, 30, 30), ambient=(0.8, 0.8, 0.8))
    scene_mouse = make_scene_for_camera(cam, bg_color=(0, 0, 0), ambient=(0.8, 0.8, 0.8))

    if cage is not None:
        tex = {"T": args.T, "B": args.B, "N": args.N, "S": args.S, "E": args.E, "W": args.W}
        Cw = camera_center_world(cam.R_wc, cam.t_wc)
        fw = camera_forward_world_cv(cam.R_wc)
        skip_face = pick_face_to_skip(Cw, fw, cage)
        add_cage_to_scene(scene_cage, cage, tex, alpha=args.cage_alpha, skip_face=skip_face)

    scenes_cage[cam.name] = scene_cage
    scenes_mouse[cam.name] = scene_mouse
    renderers[cam.name] = renderer

    # ✅ pre-render cage for EVERY camera (when mouse-on-top mode is enabled)
    if args.mouse_on_top and cage is not None:
        rgb, _ = renderer.render(scene_cage)
        pre_cage_rgb[cam.name] = rgb
```

#### 3) In the per-frame code, use `.get()` and fall back to rendering if missing
Replace:
```python
cage_rgb = pre_cage_rgb[cam.name]
```

with:
```python
cage_rgb = pre_cage_rgb.get(cam.name)
if cage_rgb is None:
    # fallback (also “self-heals” if you forgot to precompute)
    cage_rgb, _ = renderer.render(scenes_cage[cam.name])
    pre_cage_rgb[cam.name] = cage_rgb
```

That prevents the crash even if you make a setup mistake later.

---

### Quick sanity check
Right before entering the frame loop, print:

```python
print("pre_cage_rgb keys:", sorted(pre_cage_rgb.keys()))
print("camera names:", [c.name for c in cams])
```

They should match (same set of names) when `--mouse-on-top` is used and `cage is not None`.

User: I would like to discuss an extension to mouse_sim.py - without advancing to coding yet - at this point in time.

The idea is to add a second mouse. It should be able to move as the mouse currently. But of course the two mice should not intersect with their bodies. First idea would be to consider the hull or a smallest sphere including the mouse model (i.e. not the current positions body part nodes, but rather the rigid model's nodes, in order  to save compute time) that is not open for moving into for each of the two mice w.r.t. the other mouse. 

Also their should be an interaction behavior class, e.g. that the two mice have a tendency to touch each other nose to nose. And that situation (approach before touch event, touch, separate after the touch; with distance limits) would be the "social interaction" behavior - in addition to the running and rearing behaviors both mice would have individually.

Any ideas how to implement this - and what critical aspects to consider?

Assistant: A solid way to think about “Mouse 2.0 = two mice” is: **keep your current single-mouse locomotion generator**, but add a *higher-level coordination + collision* layer that turns each mouse’s “desired motion” into a *safe motion* and occasionally couples them into an interaction episode.

Below is a design that stays computationally cheap, is stable, and avoids nasty corner cases like deadlocks and jitter.

---

## 1) Collision avoidance: cheap but reliable

### Representation
Your idea—use a conservative hull computed from the *rigid rest pose*—is exactly what you want for speed and stability.

Good options in increasing realism:

1) **2D disc** in the ground plane (fastest)  
   - radius `r_body` from max distance of any rest-pose vertex/keypoint from the reference point in x–y.
   - enforce `||p1_xy - p2_xy|| >= 2*r_body + margin`.

2) **Vertical cylinder** (still cheap; helps with rearing)  
   - same disc in x–y plus height `h_body` (from mesh bbox or keypoint z extents).
   - Useful if later you want “rear” to change collision a bit (e.g., slightly smaller footprint / larger height). For now you can keep footprint constant.

3) **Multi-sphere / capsule chain** along spine (moderate cost)  
   - e.g., two or three spheres: one near head/torso, one near pelvis/tail root. Still very fast and much less conservative than a single big sphere.

**Recommendation for Mouse 1.1 with two mice:** start with **2D discs** (or two-sphere in 2D). It will look believable enough and won’t explode.

### How to enforce non-intersection
If you’re stepping at 10 fps (or more), simple discrete checks can miss “tunneling” if speed is high. Two robust approaches:

- **Velocity projection (preferred):**  
  Each time step, you compute each mouse’s desired displacement `Δp_des`. If applying both displacements would violate `d_min`, reduce/adjust them (e.g., project onto the constraint `d >= d_min`). This is stable and cheap.

- **Continuous collision check (nice-to-have):**  
  Treat relative motion as a segment and solve for closest approach. If you detect a crossing, clamp the step. Not too hard, but more math.

**Minimal projection rule (works well):**  
If after the tentative update you have overlap, push them apart along the line connecting centers, splitting the correction:
- `penetration = d_min - d`
- `p1 -= 0.5 * penetration * n`
- `p2 += 0.5 * penetration * n`
where `n = (p2-p1)/||p2-p1||`.

Then, optionally, damp their velocities a bit that step to avoid “buzzing.”

### Critical aspects to consider
- **Deadlocks in corners:** two discs can trap each other against a wall. You want wall avoidance + mild random jitter or a “yield” rule (see below).
- **Jitter/oscillation:** naive repulsion can create trembling if they keep “correcting” each frame.
- **Priority/ownership:** who yields when both want the same spot?

A simple and effective trick: give each mouse a small “dominance” scalar `w` (random per run). When resolving overlap, push more of the correction onto the lower-dominance mouse.

---

## 2) Motion architecture: “desired motion” + “steering corrections”
Keep the existing per-mouse behavior generator (run/rear) producing:
- desired heading / desired speed / desired displacement

Then add a steering layer that modifies the displacement:
- **wall repulsion**
- **mouse-mouse repulsion**
- (when interacting) **attraction to partner’s nose** + **orientation alignment**

This is classic “steering behaviors” style and avoids turning your simulator into a full planner.

### Why not do full path planning?
You *could* do A* / RRT in the cage, but it’s overkill and tends to look robotic unless you invest a lot more work.

---

## 3) Social interaction behavior: treat it as a coupled episode

If each mouse independently decides “I want to interact now,” you’ll get chaotic half-interactions. Better: define a **joint interaction episode** with phases and timers.

### Define a shared “interaction state machine”
Global interaction state (for the pair) with phases like:

0. **None** (normal behaviors run/rear independently)
1. **Approach** (move toward each other until distance threshold)
2. **Touch/Hold** (nose-to-nose within a tight range for `t_hold`)
3. **Separate** (move apart to `d_sep`, then release back to normal)

Key distances:
- `d_start`: when you consider them “engaged” (begin approach)
- `d_touch`: “touch” threshold (nose-to-nose)
- `d_sep`: separation threshold after hold
- plus `t_approach_max`, `t_hold`, `t_sep_max` timeouts

### Where do they meet?
Two easy policies:

- **One approaches, the other “waits”** (most stable)  
  Choose an “initiator” (dominance-based or random). The initiator approaches; the other slows and turns to face.

- **Both approach a meeting point** (more symmetric)  
  Meeting point = midpoint of current positions, projected into cage interior and away from walls. This prevents pushing into corners.

### The “nose-to-nose” constraint
Since you already know the nose node, define `nose_world = T_body_to_world(nose_local)`.  
During approach/hold:
- target each mouse’s body reference position and yaw so that their **nose points converge**.
- enforce a minimum body-body separation (your disc constraint) while still allowing nose proximity by making the disc radius conservative but not enormous.

### What about rearing during interaction?
Simplest and usually acceptable:
- disable rearing during interaction episodes (or reduce its probability to ~0).
- let interaction override running/rearing temporarily.

---

## 4) Arbitration between behaviors: avoid contradictions
With two independent mice plus interaction, you need a clear priority scheme. The cleanest:

### Hierarchical behavior
- **Layer 1 (global):** interaction episode active? yes/no
- **Layer 2 (per mouse):** run vs rear when not interacting
- **Layer 3 (steering):** collision/wall correction always active

Rules:
- collision avoidance **always wins** (hard constraint)
- interaction goal is next
- run/rear provides baseline motion

This avoids impossible combinations like “I must touch noses now” + “I’m rearing and cannot move forward.”

---

## 5) Recording and reproducibility
You’ll want extra protocol outputs:
- interaction episode start time, end time, initiator, partner
- touch time(s) and durations
- maybe min distance achieved

Also consider keeping seeds separated:
- `seed_mouse1`, `seed_mouse2`, `seed_interaction` derived from the master seed for reproducibility.

---

## 6) Parameters that matter most
If you implement only a few tunables, make them these:

- `r_body` (or radii per sphere if multi-sphere)
- `margin` (extra spacing)
- `interaction_rate` (expected episodes per minute)
- `d_touch`, `t_hold`, `d_sep`
- `avoid_strength` (how strongly they steer away)
- `yield_bias` or dominance

---

## 7) The main failure modes (and how to preempt them)

1) **They get stuck pushing each other into a wall.**  
   Fix: yield rule + wall repulsion + timeout that aborts interaction / forces separation direction.

2) **They oscillate near the distance constraint.**  
   Fix: apply correction to *positions* (projection) and add velocity damping; avoid pure force integration.

3) **Interaction never reaches touch because collision radius too big.**  
   Fix: define `d_touch` based on nose distance, while body collision uses a smaller/less conservative footprint (e.g., two-sphere model) or allow a smaller “interaction radius” when aligned nose-to-nose.

4) **Both try to approach simultaneously and overshoot.**  
   Fix: pick an initiator; the other becomes passive/low-speed during approach.

---

If you want a crisp next step without coding: decide whether you want **disc-only** collision or **two-sphere** collision. Two-sphere is still cheap and dramatically improves “nose touch possible while bodies don’t overlap,” which becomes important once you really emphasize nose-to-nose behavior.

User: I agree with your suggestions. And I would choose two-sphere collision.

Assistant: Great — two-sphere is the sweet spot: still cheap, but it avoids the “giant disc prevents nose contact” problem.

Here’s a concrete, implementation-ready design (still no coding), including what to store, how to compute the spheres from your existing skeleton file, how to resolve collisions robustly, and how to stage the social interaction episode.

---

## 1) Two-sphere body proxy

### Sphere centers in the mouse *local* frame
Pick two anchors that exist in your skeleton:

- **Front sphere center**: `head` (or midpoint of `head` and `nose_tip` if you want it slightly forward)
- **Rear sphere center**: `tail_root` (or midpoint of `tail_root` and pelvis-ish point; you don’t have pelvis, so tail_root is fine)

Let:
- `cF_local = head_local` (or `(head+nose)/2`)
- `cR_local = tail_root_local`

### Radii
Compute these once from the rest pose:

Option A (simple, safe):
- `rF = max ||p_i - cF|| over all nodes i`
- `rR = max ||p_i - cR|| over all nodes i`
Then multiply by a factor like `0.6–0.8` so they’re not both “whole body sized” (otherwise you’re back to disc-like conservatism).  
You can pick factor separately per sphere.

Option B (better):
Partition nodes into “front-ish” and “rear-ish” using x-coordinate in local frame (since +x is forward):
- front group: nodes with `x >= x_split`
- rear group: nodes with `x < x_split`
Then compute `rF` from front group, `rR` from rear group. This yields much tighter spheres.

**Recommendation:** use x-split at the midpoint between `head.x` and `tail_root.x`. It’s stable across models.

### Collision check in global frame
Each frame you already have per-mouse body pose (translation + yaw on the ground plane; plus rearing pitch for skeleton). For collision you can keep it cheap and **ignore rearing pitch** and just use the ground-plane pose:

For mouse m:
- `T_m`: world transform (translation + yaw only)
- `cF_world = T_m(cF_local)`
- `cR_world = T_m(cR_local)`

Between mouse A and B you have 4 sphere pairs:
- (AF,BF), (AF,BR), (AR,BF), (AR,BR)

Constraint:
\[
||c_i - c_j|| \ge r_i + r_j + margin
\]

---

## 2) Collision resolution (stable, no jitter)

### Projection-style correction (position-based)
After computing the **desired** displacements for both mice for the time step, do:

1) Apply tentative updates to both poses (yaw + translation).
2) Compute overlaps for the 4 sphere pairs.
3) If any overlap exists, resolve iteratively (2–6 iterations is usually enough):

For each overlapping pair (i,j):
- `d = ||Δ||`, `n = Δ/d`
- `pen = (r_i+r_j+margin) - d`
- apply correction along `n` to the *mice body translations*:
  - if sphere i belongs to mouse A and sphere j belongs to mouse B:
    - `pA -= wA * pen * n`
    - `pB += wB * pen * n`
where weights sum to 1. Use dominance/yielding:
- `wA = 0.5*(1-dominance_bias)` etc., or simply “initiator pushes more” during interaction.

Then damp their instantaneous speed a bit if a correction happened (prevents buzzing).

### Why not correct at sphere centers directly?
You can, but the mouse pose is your state. Best to correct translation (and optionally yaw) of the pose. Two-sphere overlap correction pushes the whole body back in a plausible way.

---

## 3) Wall constraints and wall–mouse interactions
Do the same thing against the cage walls:

- Treat each sphere center and radius against the rectangular boundaries:
  - x in [0,width], y in [0,depth]
  - enforce: `x >= r + margin`, `x <= width - r - margin`, similarly for y.

Apply corrections to the mouse translation.

This becomes important because two mice + walls is where deadlocks happen.

---

## 4) Social interaction episode (pair-level FSM)

Keep your earlier run/rear as per-mouse baselines, but add a **shared** pair state machine:

### States
- `NONE`
- `APPROACH`
- `TOUCH/HOLD`
- `SEPARATE`

### Triggering
At random times (Poisson-like) when not already interacting:
- start an episode with probability per second `λ`
- choose an initiator (dominance-weighted random)

### Approach target
To avoid corner trapping, approach a **meeting point** not too close to walls:

- `M = 0.5*(pA + pB)`  
- clamp `M` to a safe interior rectangle `[r_max+pad, width-r_max-pad] × [r_max+pad, depth-r_max-pad]`

Each mouse gets a goal:
- initiator moves toward M faster
- responder turns to face and moves slower (or “waits”)

### Touch condition
Use **nose distance** (not sphere distance):
- compute nose positions in world
- when `||noseA - noseB|| <= d_touch`, enter HOLD

### Hold
Keep them in a small distance band:
- softly steer so nose distance stays within `[d_touch_min, d_touch_max]`
- keep yaw aligned so they face each other

### Separate
Choose separation directions that avoid walls:
- e.g. move each mouse a short distance along the line connecting them, but if that points into a wall, rotate that direction by ±90°.
End separation when center distance exceeds `d_sep` (or after timeout).

### Important policy
During `APPROACH/HOLD/SEPARATE`, **disable rearing** and force “run-like” locomotion at low speed. It prevents awkward rear-while-kissing artifacts.

---

## 5) Priority / arbitration
Per time step, per mouse:

1) Determine “intent” velocity:
   - if interacting: goal-driven velocity (approach/hold/separate)
   - else: your existing random track running or rearing

2) Apply steering corrections:
   - wall projection
   - two-sphere projection (mouse-mouse)
   - (optional) slight random perturbation if stuck

3) Update pose and skeleton.

---

## 6) What to log (you’ll thank yourself later)
- per frame: behavior of each mouse (run/rear/social if you treat social as overriding)
- protocol CSV for pair episodes:
  - start time, end time
  - initiator id
  - touch time(s), hold duration
  - min nose distance achieved
  - any abort reasons (timeout, stuck)

---

## 7) Parameter sanity defaults
- `margin`: 5–10 mm (depending on units)
- `d_touch`: 10–20 mm (nose proximity; tune visually)
- `t_hold`: 0.3–1.5 s
- `d_sep`: (2*r_body + margin) + 20 mm
- interaction rate: e.g. 2–6 episodes/minute

---

## 8) The one tricky conceptual point
**Two-sphere collision can still block nose touch** if your front sphere is too big or centered too far back. So:
- center front sphere slightly forward (toward nose),
- compute radii from a front-only node subset.

This is the main “calibration lever” to make social interaction look plausible without allowing interpenetration.

---

When you’re ready to move from design to code, the cleanest way is:
- introduce a `MouseAgent` class (pose, behavior state, RNG, goal),
- a `PairController` (interaction FSM + dominance),
- a `CollisionSystem` (walls + two-sphere projection).

## What’s in `mouse_sim2.py`

### Two mice
- Two agents (`mouse_id = 0, 1`) each have their own:
  - `TrackState` (x, y, theta, v, omega)
  - `BehaviorState` (run/rear, gait phase, rearing params)

### Two-sphere collision proxy (fast, less conservative than one big disc)
- **Front sphere** center: by default midpoint of (head, nose) in local coords  
- **Rear sphere** center: tail_root in local coords  
- Radii are computed from the rest-pose node cloud using an x-split and max XY distance.

Collision constraints enforced each frame by **position-based projection**:
- all sphere-pairs separated by `r_i + r_j + margin`
- each sphere stays inside cage boundaries

### Social interaction as a coupled episode (pair-level FSM)
Global `SocialState` has phases:
1. `NONE`
2. `APPROACH`
3. `HOLD`
4. `SEPARATE`

During social:
- per-mouse run/rear is **frozen** and overridden by `behavior = 2` (“social”)
- heading + speed are steered toward meeting/partner goals
- rearing is suppressed (by design)

### Hierarchical arbitration
Per frame:
1) produce desired motion (random running or rearing unless social)  
2) if social active: override with social steering targets  
3) apply **soft avoidance steering** away from the other mouse  
4) apply **hard projection constraints** (walls + 2-sphere non-overlap)  
5) generate skeleton pose and write outputs

---

## Outputs
In `--out-dir` you’ll now get:

- `coords_3d.csv`  
  Columns: `frame,time,mouse_id,behavior,node,x,y,z`  
  (`behavior` is 0 running, 1 rearing, 2 social)

- `keypoints_<cam>.csv`  
  Columns: `frame,time,mouse_id,behavior,node,u,v,visible`

- `behavior_protocol.csv`  
  Per-mouse `0 -> 1` entries (only when **not** in social)

- `interaction_protocol.csv`  
  One row per social episode with:
  `start`, optional `touch`, `end`, `initiator`, `end_reason`, `min_nose_dist`

- Videos (optional): skeletons drawn in different colors for the two mice.

```bash
  --write-video \
  --duration 60 --fps 10 --seed 2 \
  --out-dir out2m
```

## Parameters you’ll likely tune first

### To make nose-to-nose easier (without overlap)
- `--proxy-scale` (default 0.85)  
  Lower → smaller collision spheres → easier close contact  
  Example: `--proxy-scale 0.75`

- `--collision-margin` (default 5.0)  
  Lower → less clearance between bodies

- `--social-touch-dist` (default 18.0)  
  Increase slightly if your mesh/keypoints put noses farther apart visually.

### Social frequency / feel
- `--social-rate-per-min` (default 3.0) (0 disables social)
- `--social-hold-time` (default 1.0)
- `--social-approach-speed`, `--social-separate-speed`

---

If you want the social episode to be *more symmetric* (both move equally instead of initiator/responder), say so and I’ll adjust the approach steering rule (still within the same structure).

Assistant: Yep — the reason you saw **no interaction** is that the original social scheduler was purely Poisson (`expovariate`) *plus* a minimum-distance gate. That combination can still produce “no social episode” in a short run (or if the min-distance gate blocks starts repeatedly).

Even with the new defaults, if you want “I want to see it immediately, no excuses”, run e.g.:

User: Are there any changes necessary in order to run mouse_deform_render.py on the new simulation data with 2 mice?

Assistant: Yes — unless your current `mouse_deform_render.py` already *explicitly* supports a `mouse_id` column, you’ll need **some adjustment**. The new `mouse_sim2.py` writes **two mice into the same `coords_3d.csv`**, so the renderer has to either:

1) **split the CSV per mouse** (no renderer changes), or  
2) **render both mice in one pass** (small renderer change).

Below are both options.

---

## Option 0 (no code changes): split the 2-mouse CSV into two 1-mouse CSVs

This is the fastest way to confirm everything works.

### Using `python` (pandas)
```bash
python - <<'PY'
import pandas as pd
df = pd.read_csv("out3mesh/coords_3d.csv")
for mid in sorted(df["mouse_id"].unique()):
    out = f"out3mesh/coords_3d_mouse{mid}.csv"
    df[df["mouse_id"]==mid].drop(columns=["mouse_id"]).to_csv(out, index=False)
    print("wrote", out)
PY
```

```bash

python mouse_deform_render.py ... --coords-3d out3mesh/coords_3d_mouse1.csv --out-dir out_render_m1
```

This will give you two separate video sets (one mouse each). If you later want a single combined video, you can composite them — but that’s extra work.

## Option 1 (recommended): update `mouse_deform_render.py` to render both mice

### What must change
#### 1) CSV reader: accept `mouse_id` (backward compatible)
- If `mouse_id` exists: group by `(frame, mouse_id)`
- If not: assume `mouse_id = 0` for all rows

Data structure you want per frame:
```text
frames[frame][mouse_id] = {
  "behavior": int,
  "nodes": { node_name: (x,y,z), ... }
}
```

#### 2) Per-frame deformation: run once per mouse
For each frame:
- for each `mouse_id` in that frame:
  - build target keypoint array from that mouse’s node coords
  - compute the RBF deformation for the mesh
  - add that deformed mesh to the scene

That is literally “do what you already do once, but twice”.

#### 3) Rendering: add both meshes before `renderer.render(...)`
In your “physical” rendering branch (cage + mouse):
- add all submeshes for mouse 0
- add all submeshes for mouse 1
- render once
- remove all nodes

In `--mouse-on-top` mode:
- cage pass stays the same
- “mouse-only pass” should add **both mice meshes** before rendering; your existing depth-mask composition still works (mask = depth > 0).

#### 4) Optional: make mice distinguishable
Not required, but helpful:
- multiply base color by a per-mouse tint, or
- apply different `metallicFactor/roughnessFactor`, or
- draw one slightly darker

You can do it without textures by setting a `pyrender.MetallicRoughnessMaterial(baseColorFactor=...)` per mouse.

### Performance note
With two mice you roughly double the per-frame deformation cost.

If you’re using exact TPS RBF with the *same source landmarks* (`mesh_nodes`) each time, you can speed it up by:
- precomputing the TPS system matrix factorization once
- solving only for new RHS (the target positions) per mouse per frame

You don’t have to do this first, but it’s the correct optimization once you scale duration/fps.

## What does *not* need to change
- `cameras.json` can remain identical: the cameras and cage are scene-level, not mouse-level.
- `--mesh` and `--mesh-nodes` are reused for both mice (it’s the same mesh deformed into two poses).

## Sanity checks (when you implement Option 1)
If something looks wrong, these checks isolate the problem quickly:

1) For each `(frame, mouse_id)`, verify you have **all required nodes** (head, tail_root, nose, etc.). Missing nodes → RBF becomes ill-posed or collapses.
2) Confirm both mice’s world positions are inside the cage bounds.
3) If one mouse “teleports” to the other: you accidentally mixed rows from different mouse_id in the grouping.

User: So now we have two mice with 9 body parts each. And the mice show independent behaviors 0: running, 1: rearing, and a joint behavior 2:interacting. The simulated data (.csv files) allows to display an ethogram of the behaviors - as often done in animal behavior analysis.

The uploaded file is the program you previously suggested for deep-learned and conventional behavior analysis of a single mouse.

Please adapt it to the analysis of the joint behavior of the both mice. What are the changes that are required. Before coding please make suggestions and comments. Which decisions are required?

Assistant: You can adapt your existing `train_behavior.py` fileciteturn6file0 to “pair / joint behavior” without changing the overall training scaffold (runs → windows → sequence model → per-frame labels). The key changes are: **(a) what constitutes one training sample**, **(b) what the target label(s) are**, and **(c) how to make the representation sensible / invariant** for two animals.

Below are the design decisions you need to make, plus what would change in the code for each decision.

---

## 1) What exactly is the prediction target?

You now have per-frame data with:
- two mice (IDs 0/1),
- individual behaviors {0 running, 1 rearing},
- and a joint episode behavior {2 interacting} (typically both mice get label 2 during the episode).

There are three reasonable label designs:

### A) **Binary “interaction vs not” (pair-level)**
- `y_pair[t] = 1` if **either** mouse is in behavior 2 at frame t, else 0.
- Easiest, robust, closest to “ethogram of social interaction”.

**Code impact:** model output changes from 2 classes → 2 classes (still 2, but different meaning), minimal.

### B) **Multi-class pair ethogram**
Define pair state per frame as:
- `0`: both running
- `1`: mouse0 rear, mouse1 run
- `2`: mouse0 run, mouse1 rear
- `3`: both rear
- `4`: interacting (joint)

This yields a single per-frame label `y_pair[t] ∈ {0..4}`.

**Code impact:** model output dim becomes 5; baseline changes accordingly.

### C) **Multi-task (recommended long-term)**
Predict:
- `y_interact[t] ∈ {0,1}` (pair-level)
- `y0[t] ∈ {run,rear}` and `y1[t] ∈ {run,rear}` but only when not interacting, or with masking.

This matches your hierarchical semantics: “interaction overrides individual behavior”.

**Code impact:** model head(s) produce 1 binary + 2×binary, plus a loss mask. More work, but it avoids forcing a single label to represent everything.

**Decision required:** Do you want “just interaction detection” now (A), or a full ethogram (B), or the hierarchical decomposition (C)?  
If you want a fast first working version, I’d do **A** first, then **C**.

---

## 2) What is one “Run” now?

Your current `Run` is for one mouse: `pos(T,K,3), y(T)` fileciteturn6file0.

For two mice you need either:

### Option 1 (clean): **Run holds both mice**
- `pos: (T, M=2, K, 3)`
- `y_ind: (T, M)` with values {0,1,2}
- derived `y_pair: (T,)` depending on label design

This is the most direct and avoids awkward “two runs per file” splitting.

### Option 2 (hacky): treat each mouse as a separate Run and stitch later
You’ll fight label alignment and windowing; not recommended.

**Decision required:** use Option 1 (I recommend it).

---

## 3) How to represent the two-mouse pose per frame?

This is the most important modeling choice. If you just concatenate two body-normalized feature vectors, the network *can* learn, but generalization and stability depend on invariances.

You have three representation styles:

### A) Concatenate per-mouse body frames + relative features (practical default)
1) Compute body-frame normalization for each mouse separately (your existing `body_frame_normalize`) fileciteturn6file0  
2) Extract features per mouse: positions (+ velocities).
3) Add explicit pair geometry features in cage/world frame, e.g.:
- `Δroot = root1 - root0` (xy and maybe z)
- `nose-nose distance`
- relative heading angle (from each mouse’s head-root vector)
- maybe a few key inter-point distances (nose-to-head, nose-to-tailroot)

Then `X[t] = concat(X0[t], X1[t], rel[t])`.

**Pros:** easy, strong baseline, works with your existing models.  
**Cons:** not permutation-invariant (mouse0 vs mouse1 ordering matters).

### B) Pair-centric frame (more invariant)
Define a “pair coordinate frame” per frame:
- origin = midpoint of the two tail_roots (or body reference points)
- x-axis = direction from mouse0 tail_root to mouse1 tail_root

Express all keypoints of both mice in this frame. This makes the representation invariant to global translation and rotation automatically.

**Pros:** very clean invariance.  
**Cons:** if mice cross / swap roles, the frame can flip unless you enforce a consistent ordering.

### C) Permutation-invariant features (best if you expect identity swaps later)
If you anticipate real data where tracking IDs can swap, you want either:
- **data augmentation**: randomly swap mouse0/mouse1 in training 50% of the time, and require labels to swap appropriately, or
- **symmetric features**: use `(X0+X1, X0−X1)` and/or set-based pooling.

**Decision required:** In simulation, IDs are stable, but if you plan to move to real recordings, decide whether to bake in invariance now. A cheap compromise: do (A) plus **swap augmentation**.

---

## 4) What to do with the “interaction overrides run/rear” hierarchy?

This affects labels and loss.

- If you pick **binary interaction detection (A)**, it’s simple: only care about interaction.
- If you pick **multi-class pair ethogram (B)**, you implicitly encode hierarchy: class 4 supersedes others.
- If you pick **multi-task (C)**, you should mask individual losses during interaction frames:
  - `loss = loss_interact + mask*(loss_mouse0 + loss_mouse1)`

**Decision required:** Do you want the training objective to respect that hierarchy explicitly (C), or keep it implicit (B)?

---

## 5) Class imbalance and evaluation

Interaction is typically rarer than “not interacting”, so you’ll likely get:
- high accuracy by predicting “no interaction”
- poor F1 for interaction

Changes you’ll want:
- **weighted cross-entropy** (or `pos_weight` for binary BCE)
- report **F1(interaction)** and maybe **balanced accuracy**
- maybe temporal smoothing in post-processing (a small hysteresis) for ethograms

**Decision required:** primary metric = F1 for interaction, or macro-F1 across all states?

---

## 6) Baselines worth adding for the pair case (very important)
Your current baseline is logistic regression on flattened pose (+vel) fileciteturn6file0. For social interaction you should also add a “physics prior” baseline:

### Rule-based baseline (strong and interpretable)
Predict interaction if:
- `d(nose0, nose1) < τ_dist`
- and both are facing each other: dot(forward0, forward_to_other) > τ_angle and dot(forward1, forward_to_other) < -τ_angle
- optionally, distance between body centers below some threshold

This will be surprisingly competitive and gives you a sanity check that the simulation labels are learnable.

**Decision required:** include this rule baseline (I’d strongly recommend yes).

---

## Summary of required decisions (minimal set)

1) **Label type**: binary interaction vs not (fast) OR full 5-class pair ethogram OR multi-task hierarchy.  
2) **Representation**: concat per-mouse body frames + explicit relative features (fast) vs pair-frame coordinates (more invariant).  
3) **Identity handling**: rely on stable IDs OR add swap augmentation / invariant design.  
4) **Imbalance strategy**: weighted loss + F1(interaction) as headline metric.  
5) **Baseline**: include a geometric rule baseline (nose distance + facing).

---

- multi-mouse CSV loader (`mouse_id`),
- pair feature builder,
- baseline rule + baseline logreg,
- LSTM / Transformer / xLSTM variants with correct output heads,
- and an evaluation report suitable for an ethogram (per-frame + optional smoothed).

1) multi-task hiearchy
2) pair-frame coordinates
3) swap augmentation/invariant design
4) weighted loss + F1
5) geometric rule baseline

Assistant: Given your choices, the adaptation is conceptually straightforward: **turn the “single-mouse per-run sequence classifier” into a “pair run” multitask sequence classifier** with (i) a **pair-centric coordinate frame**, (ii) **ID-swap augmentation**, (iii) a **3-head network**, (iv) **masked/weighted losses**, plus (v) a **geometric rule baseline** for interaction.

Your current `train_behavior.py` is single-mouse: it loads `pos(T,K,3)` + `y(T)` from `coords_3d.csv`, normalizes into a body frame (`tail_root`→`head`), makes features (pos±vel), then trains either baseline logreg or a deep model that outputs 2 logits per frame (rear vs run). fileciteturn7file0 The changes below map directly onto that structure.

---

## 1) Data model changes: from `Run` to `PairRun`

### Current
- `Run.pos`: `(T, K, 3)`
- `Run.y`: `(T,)` (0/1)

### New (pair)
- `PairRun.pos`: `(T, M=2, K, 3)`
- `PairRun.y`: `(T, M=2)` with values in `{0,1,2}` (run, rear, interact)

From this, derive multitask labels per frame `t`:

- **Interaction (pair-level)**  
  `y_int[t] = 1 if (y[t,0]==2 or y[t,1]==2) else 0`

- **Individual (masked by non-interaction)**  
  `mask[t] = 1 if y_int[t]==0 else 0`  
  `y0[t] = 1 if y[t,0]==1 else 0` (rear vs run), used only where mask=1  
  `y1[t] = 1 if y[t,1]==1 else 0`, used only where mask=1

**Why this matches your hierarchy:** interaction is predicted always; individual behaviors are only trained/evaluated when not interacting (exactly the “override” semantics you described).

### Loader impact
Your current loader expects CSV columns `frame,time,behavior,node,x,y,z`. fileciteturn7file2  
The 2-mouse sim writes `mouse_id` too. The loader must:
- group rows by `(frame, mouse_id)`
- fill `pos[t, mouse_id, node, :]`
- set `y[t, mouse_id]` from the group’s `behavior`

Backward compatibility: if `mouse_id` missing, assume all rows are `mouse_id=0` and error if the user requested pair training.

---

## 2) Feature representation: pair-frame coordinates (your choice)

### Pair frame definition (per frame)
Let `r0 = tail_root(mouse0)` and `r1 = tail_root(mouse1)` in world coords.

- **Origin**: `o = 0.5*(r0 + r1)`
- **x-axis**: direction from mouse0→mouse1 in the ground plane  
  `x̂ = normalize([r1.x - r0.x, r1.y - r0.y, 0])` (fallback to `[1,0,0]` if near-zero)
- **z-axis**: `[0,0,1]`
- **y-axis**: `ŷ = ẑ × x̂` (right-handed)

Transform any world point `p` to pair frame:
- `p' = Rᵀ (p - o)` where `R=[x̂ ŷ ẑ]`

### What goes into X[t]
At minimum:
- all keypoints of both mice in pair frame: shape `(2*K*3,)` flattened.

Useful additions (especially for interaction):
- velocities in pair frame (finite differences): another `(2*K*3,)`
- relative scalars: `||nose0 - nose1||`, `||r0 - r1||`, and facing dot-products (see baseline)

This replaces your current per-mouse body normalization + flatten. fileciteturn7file10

---

## 3) Swap augmentation / invariance (your choice)

You want robustness to identity swapping. With a supervised per-mouse output head, the clean version is:

### Training-time augmentation (50% probability)
For each sampled window:
- swap the two mice in the input: `pos[:,0] ↔ pos[:,1]`
- swap the individual labels: `y0 ↔ y1`
- interaction label unchanged.

Because the pair frame is defined using “mouse0→mouse1”, swapping also **flips the frame** (x-axis reverses). That’s fine: the model sees both orientations and learns a symmetric decision boundary.

**Optional extra invariance trick (often helps):**
append symmetric/antisymmetric pooled features:
- `S = feats0 + feats1`
- `D = feats1 - feats0`  
and concatenate `[feats0, feats1, S, D]`.  
This makes the invariance more “available” to the network, but it’s not strictly necessary if you do swap augmentation.

---

## 4) Model change: one backbone, three heads (multitask)

Your current deep models output `(B,T,2)` logits (rear vs run). fileciteturn7file9

New output should be:

- `logit_int`: `(B,T,1)`  (interaction yes/no)
- `logit0`: `(B,T,1)`  (mouse0 rear yes/no)
- `logit1`: `(B,T,1)`  (mouse1 rear yes/no)

Use the same backbone choices as before:
- LSTM / Transformer / xLSTM: keep architecture, replace final head with 3 heads.

---

## 5) Loss: weighted + masked + F1 reporting (your choice)

### Losses
Use `BCEWithLogitsLoss` (more natural for binary heads) with **pos_weight**:

- Interaction loss over all frames:
  - `L_int = BCE(logit_int, y_int, pos_weight_int)`
- Individual losses only over non-interaction frames:
  - `L0 = BCE(logit0[mask], y0[mask], pos_weight0)`
  - `L1 = BCE(logit1[mask], y1[mask], pos_weight1)`

Total:
- `L = L_int + λ*(L0 + L1)/2`  
(λ is a tunable, default 1.0)

### Class weights / pos_weight computation
Compute from *training runs only*:
- `pos_weight_int = N_neg / N_pos` for interaction
- similarly for rearing on masked frames.

### Metrics
Report at least:
- **F1(interaction)** on test frames
- **F1(rearing)** for mouse0 and mouse1 on masked frames (and their mean)

If you want an ethogram-looking output later, you can add optional temporal smoothing on predicted interaction (hysteresis or majority filter), but that’s post-processing, not training.

---

## 6) Baselines (your choice includes geometric rule baseline)

### A) Geometric rule baseline for interaction (strong sanity check)
Using *pair-frame or world-frame*, compute per frame:

- `d = ||nose0 - nose1||`
- facing vectors (world):
  - `f0 = normalize(head0 - tail_root0)`
  - `f1 = normalize(head1 - tail_root1)`
- direction-to-partner:
  - `g0 = normalize(nose1 - nose0)`
  - `g1 = normalize(nose0 - nose1)`

Predict interaction if:
- `d < τ_dist`
- and `dot(f0, g0) > τ_face` and `dot(f1, g1) > τ_face`

**Decisions to set as CLI defaults (can be tuned from train set):**
- `τ_dist` in mm (or your units)
- `τ_face` e.g. 0.3–0.7

This baseline is important because it tells you quickly whether your synthetic interaction episodes are separable in simple geometry.

### B) Classical baseline for the multitask (logreg)
Your existing baseline is logistic regression on per-frame flattened features. fileciteturn7file7  
For multitask you’ll do *three* independent classifiers:
- LogReg for interaction (all frames)
- LogReg for mouse0 rearing (masked frames)
- LogReg for mouse1 rearing (masked frames)

Each can use the same per-frame features you feed the deep model (pair-frame flatten + optional vel).

(And you can still apply your `--smooth-k` majority smoothing per run, like you already do. fileciteturn7file7)

---

## 7) Remaining small decisions (I can pick sensible defaults if you don’t care)

1) **Which nodes define the pair frame and facing?**  
   - pair frame: `tail_root` is ideal (stable)  
   - facing: `head - tail_root`  
   - interaction distance: `nose_tip` to `nose_tip`

2) **Windowing**  
   Your default window=20 at 10 fps is 2 seconds. For interaction detection, 1–2 seconds is fine. If you later increase fps, you might want to define windows in seconds and convert to frames.

3) **Units / scaling**  
   If you’re in mm, raw coordinates are larger; models usually handle it, but standardization (baseline) and normalization (deep) benefit from consistent scaling. We can add `--scale-mm` (or just standardize features inside the model pipeline).

4) **Whether to include velocities**  
   Usually helps (approach/leave dynamics), so keep `use_vel=True` by default.

---

If you confirm the node labels you want to use for **(tail_root, head, nose_tip)** (just to avoid a mismatch like `nose` vs `nose_tip`), I’ll implement the full updated script as `train_behavior_pair.py` (or overwrite `train_behavior.py`, your choice) with:
- multi-mouse CSV loader,
- pair-frame feature builder,
- swap augmentation,
- multitask heads and masked weighted losses,
- F1 reporting,
- geometric rule baseline + logreg multitask baseline,
- LSTM/Transformer/xLSTM options preserved.

It implements exactly what you chose:

### 3) xLSTM (if installed)

```bash
python train_behavior_pair.py --coords-3d out2m/coords_3d.csv --model xlstm --amp --tf32 --xlstm-backend cuda
# if cuda backend fails:
python train_behavior_pair.py --coords-3d out2m/coords_3d.csv --model xlstm --amp --tf32 --xlstm-backend native
```

### 4) Geometric rule baseline (interaction only)
```bash
python train_behavior_pair.py \
  --coords-3d out2m/coords_3d.csv \
  --model baseline_rule \
  --rule-dist 20 --rule-face 0.4
```

### 5) Classical baseline (3× logistic regression)
```bash
python train_behavior_pair.py --coords-3d out2m/coords_3d.csv --model baseline_logreg
```

```bash

  --coords-3d out_pair/coords_3d.csv \
  --model lstm \
  --out-dir out_pair_train \
  --amp --tf32
```

Outputs in `out_pair_train/`:
- `ethogram_gt_*.csv` (GT for the test split)
- `ethogram_pred_*.csv` (predicted ethograms)

User: The train_behavior_pair.py splits into train and val, right? How is that done? 

Please also code a predict_behavior_pair.py that reads the resulting trained model (e.g. .ckpt) and uses it for inference only. If train_behavior_pair.py has to be changed, e.g. to store the resulting model, please do so.

Assistant: ### 1) Does `train_behavior_pair.py` split into train + val? How?

**It splits into “train” and “test” (used as validation during training)**—there is no separate third “final test” split.

The split happens in `split_runs(...)`:

- **If you provide ≥2 simulation runs** (i.e., multiple `--coords-3d` files):
  - It **shuffles the runs** with `--seed` and selects `round(test_frac * num_runs)` whole runs as the **held-out set**.
  - This avoids leakage because **entire runs** are held out.

- **If you provide only 1 run**:
  - It does a **time split** inside that run:
    - first `(1 - test_frac)` fraction of frames → train
    - last `test_frac` fraction → held-out set (again used like validation)

So: *the held-out subset is evaluated every epoch*, so it behaves like a **validation** set; it’s just called “test” in the code.

## What will be changed (precisely) when implemented

### A) Changes to `train_behavior_pair.py`
Add CLI args:
- `--out-dir` (default e.g. `behavior_pair_out`)
- `--ckpt-prefix` (default e.g. `behavior_pair`)
- `--no-save-best`, `--no-save-last`

During training:
- after each epoch, compute validation metrics (F1 interaction, etc.)
- save:
  - `<prefix>_best.ckpt` when **interaction F1 improves**
  - `<prefix>_last.ckpt` each epoch (optional)
- save `<prefix>_config.json` including:
  - model type + architecture hyperparams
  - `in_dim`
  - node labels (tail_root/head/nose)
  - `window`, `use_vel`, `scale_mm`
  - `node_order` used in training (so inference preserves ordering)

### B) `predict_behavior_pair.py`
CLI:
- `--ckpt path/to/best.ckpt`
- `--coords-3d ...`
- `--out-dir pred_out`
- `--device cpu|cuda`
- optional overrides: `--scale-mm`, `--smooth-k`, `--thr-int`, `--thr-rear`

Behavior:
- build exact same model from `config`
- load weights
- run sliding-window inference (same as training evaluation)
- export:
  - `ethogram_pred_<run>.csv` (probabilities + decoded states)
  - `ethogram_gt_<run>.csv` if GT behavior exists in CSV

---

## Why your inference script needs the config JSON
Because reconstruction of the model depends on:
- backbone type (lstm/transformer/xlstm)
- hidden sizes / layers
- feature dimension `in_dim`
- node label mapping / ordering
- window length (xLSTM uses context length)

So checkpoint must include config (or a separate config file must exist). Best practice: store both inside the ckpt and also dump JSON.

Assistant: You should **start a new chat for the LaTeX paper**.

Not because a paper is “too heavy”, but because in a long thread like this, the risk is **context mis-selection**: details get buried, filenames drift (`train_behavior.py` vs `train_behavior_pair.py`, multiple versions of `mouse_sim2.py`, different renderer variants), and I might accidentally describe the wrong version or omit an important design choice you made. A fresh chat lets you control exactly what is “in scope” and makes the resulting paper cleaner and more reproducible.

### Do this in the new chat (best practice)
Upload (or paste) only the final artifacts you want the paper to describe:

1) `mouse_sim.py` (single mouse) and **final** `mouse_sim2.py` (two mice + social)
2) `mouse_deform_render_multi_swaprb.py` (or whichever renderer is final)
3) `train_behavior_pair.py` (the one you actually ran)
4) A short “experiment manifest” text (5–10 lines), e.g.:
   - cage size + units
   - fps/duration
   - seeds used
   - which behaviors exist and their labels
   - which camera layout
   - which model options you consider “main” (LSTM, Transformer, xLSTM)

And then ask for the paper.

### What I can produce for you in one shot
In that new chat, I can generate:
- a complete LaTeX source (`paper.tex`) with:
  - abstract, intro, methods (simulation, deformation rendering, learning), results, discussion
  - algorithm boxes / pseudocode
  - parameter tables
  - a section on limitations and next steps (mesh + RBF, real data, tracking swaps, etc.)
- plus a BibTeX file with a minimal set of citations (PyTorch, TPS/RBF, LSTM/Transformer, pose estimation / ethograms)

### One thing to keep here
Before you switch chats, copy this single sentence into your new request because it’s the key “thesis”:

> “We built a synthetic two-mouse 3D skeleton simulator with running/rearing and coupled interaction episodes, rendered it via RBF mesh deformation into multi-camera videos, and trained multitask sequence models in a pair-centric frame to infer interaction and individual behaviors.”

That will anchor the narrative.
